The Fourfold Classification of Matter (Hayula)
The author enumerates four types of hayula with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct meanings of hayula (matter) in Islamic hylomorphic theory and how each relates to corporeal substances.

Core Idea
Matter has four layers, from prime matter to composed substrates.

In this passage, the author distinguishes four senses of 'matter' (hayula) by analyzing how each serves as a substratum for forms. This classification is crucial for understanding hylomorphic composition in Islamic philosophy. Let's explore each type with its definition and example.

Guided Path
1
Read the general definition of hayula
Start by reading the first sentence where hayula is defined as the substratum of the substantial form. This sets the context for the four divisions.
Hayula in general is the receptive principle for form.
2
Identify the first matter (al-hayula al-ula)
Notice that the first matter is described as a non-corporeal substance (jawhar ghayr jism). It is the substratum for what is 'connected by itself'—the form of corporeality.
This is prime matter, pure potentiality.
3
Distinguish the second matter (al-hayula al-thaniyah)
The second matter is a body (jism) that already possesses a form. It serves as substrate for additional specific forms, like the human body for the soul.
This is the body itself as a composite.
4
Understand the third and fourth matters
The third matter is a natural body with its specific form that becomes substrate for an artifact form (e.g., wood for a bed). The fourth matter is a composite of body and two forms serving as substrate for a higher form (e.g., limbs for the whole body).
These are more complex material substrates.
5
Summarize the categorization
Review the final summary: the first matter is a part of the body, the second matter is the body itself, and the last two are parts of their respective composites.
This clarifies how each relates to the concept of 'body'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الهيولى على الإطلاق هي محل الصورة الجوهرية وهي أربعة أقسام الهيولى الأولى : وهو جوهر غير جسم محل المتصل بذاته. والهيولى الثانية : هو جسم قام به صورة كالأجسام بالنسبة إلى صورها النوعية. والهيولى الثالثة : وهي الأجسام مع صورها النوعية التي صارت محلاً لصورة أخرى كالخشب لصورة السرير والطين لصورة الكوز. والهيولى الرابعة : وهي أن يكون الجسم مع الصورتين محلاً لصورة أخرى كالأعضاء لصورة البدن وأجزاء البيت لصورته. فالهيولى الأولى جزء الجسم هو جزء والثانية نفس الجسم والأخيران جزءاً لهما
Know that hayula (matter) in an absolute sense is the substratum of the substantial form (al-surah al-jawhariyyah). And it is of four divisions: The first matter (al-hayula al-ula): it is a non-corporeal substance (jawhar ghayr jism), the substratum of that which is connected by itself (al-muttasil bi-dhatihi). The second matter (al-hayula al-thaniyah): it is a body (jism) in which a form resides, like bodies in relation to their specific forms. The third matter (al-hayula al-thalithah): it is bodies with their specific forms that have become a substratum for another form, like wood for the form of a bed, and clay for the form of a pot. The fourth matter (al-hayula al-rabi'ah): it is that a body with two forms becomes a substratum for another form, like limbs for the form of a body, and parts of a house for the form of the house. So the first matter is a part of the body; the second matter is the body itself; and the last two are parts of their composites.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the concept of hayula (matter) into four types. The author begins by defining hayula in general as the substratum of the substantial form. He then enumerates four divisions. The first matter (al-hayula al-ula) is a non-corporeal substance that underlies what is connected by itself—that is, it is the prime matter that receives the form of corporeality. The second matter is a body that already has a form, so it serves as matter for further specific forms (e.g., the human body for its soul). The third matter is a body with its specific form that then becomes the matter for another composite, like wood for a bed. The fourth matter is a body with two forms that serves as matter for yet another form, like limbs for the body as a whole. The author then summarizes: the first matter is a part of the body (its material component), the second matter is the body itself (as a composite), and the last two are parts of their respective composites (they are the material components of higher-level compounds). This classification clarifies different senses of 'matter' in hylomorphic theory.

Key Terms
hayula (هيولى)
surah jawhariyyah (صورة جوهرية)
jawhar ghayr jism (جوهر غير جسم)
al-hayula al-ula (الهيولى الأولى)
al-hayula al-thaniyah (الهيولى الثانية)
al-hayula al-thalithah (الهيولى الثالثة)
al-hayula al-rabi'ah (الهيولى الرابعة)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hayula always refers to prime matter, the most basic substrate.
Correction: This classification shows that hayula can refer to more composite substrates, such as a body already possessing forms.

Checkpoint
Which type of hayula is a non-corporeal substance that serves as the substratum for the corporeal form?
A) The second matter
B) The third matter
C) The first matter
D) The fourth matter

Key Takeaway
Hayula is not a single concept; it spans four levels from pure potentiality to already-formed substrates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are composed of matter at multiple levels: your body is hayula for your soul. Which of the four types of hayula best describes your physical body right now? How does that affect your understanding of your own mortality and purpose?
?
The first hayula is pure potentiality. In what areas of your life are you still unformed potential? What one concrete step will you take today to actualize that potential into a form that pleases your Creator?

Proof of Hylomorphic Composition from Connection and Separation
Iji proves body is composed of matter and form via alternation of connection and separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the alternation of connection and separation in a body proves that it is composed of a permanent matter (hayula) and a transient form (surah).

Core Idea
A changing connection proves a permanent substratum distinct from it.

Iji uses a simple observation: when a connected body separates, its connection is lost but something must endure to receive the new state. This enduring receiver cannot be the connection itself because it disappears. Through this reasoning, we arrive at the concepts of matter (hayula) and form (surah), the fundamental constituents of every body. In this lesson, we will walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Observe the phenomenon of separation
Consider a connected body that becomes separated. The original connection is lost. What remains? The passage argues there must be something that was receptive to both connection and separation.
This is the starting point of the proof.
2
Identify the substratum
That which receives both connection and separation cannot be the connection itself, because the connection ceases when separation occurs. It must be something else.
The substratum is distinct from both states.
3
Name the substratum and form
The passage calls the enduring substratum 'primary matter' (hayula) and the connection 'form' (surah). Their composite is 'body' (jism).
4
Understand the analogy with quantities
Iji draws an analogy: just as changing quantities imply a permanent form that receives them, changing connections imply a permanent matter that receives them.
This reinforces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسم المتصل إذا طرأ عليه الانفصال زال اتصاله وصار منفصلاً وحينئذ نقول : ( فإن ثمة أمراً قابلاً للاتصال تارة والانفصال أخرى و) ذلك (القابل لهما ليس نفس الاتصال ضرورة أن القابل الثابت للشيئين) اللذين يزول كل منهما مع حصول الآخر ( غير كل واحد من الشيئين ( المتزايلين)، فالقابل للاتصال والانفصال يغاير كلا منهما ( أو نقول : قابل الاتصال والانفصال، (باق مع الانفصال والاتصال لا يبقى مع الانفصال، فهو غيره ) أي قابل الاتصال والانفصال غير الاتصال، وكيف لا ؟ والشيء لا يكون قابلاً لنفسه ولا لما ينافيه ( فهذا الأمر الذي هو قابل للانفصال ومغاير للاتصال ( هو الذي نسميه (بالهيولى) الأولى التي تحل فيها الصورة الجسمية، فإنه كان قبل طريان الانفصال متصفاً بالاتصال الواحد حيث كان متصلاً واحداً، وبعده متصفاً بانفصال بل باتصالين حادثين عنده، حيث كان حينئذ متصلين. وتلخيصه أنهم كما أثبتوا بتوارد (المقادير) المختلفة على الجسم ( مع بقاء صورة ) جوهرية (اتصالية قابلة للكميات ( المتواردة، كون الكم المتغير (غير الاتصال ) الباقي بحاله (أثبتوا أيضاً (بتوارد اتصالات مختلفة بالشخص على أمر باق) على حاله ( بالضرورة كون الاتصال ( المتبدل ( غير ما يقابله وسموا الاتصال صورة والقابل له (مادة والمركب منهما جسماً
When separation occurs to the connected body, its connection ceases and it becomes separated. Then we say: 'There is something that is receptive to connection sometimes and to separation at other times.' And that which is receptive to both is not the connection itself, necessarily because that which is permanently receptive to two things that each cease when the other occurs is other than each of the two mutually exclusive things. So that which is receptive to connection and separation is other than each of them. Or we say: That which is receptive to connection and separation remains with separation, while connection does not remain with separation, so it is other than it. That is, that which is receptive to connection and separation is other than connection. And how could it not be? For a thing is not receptive to itself nor to what is incompatible with it. So this thing, which is receptive to separation and other than connection, is what we call the primary matter (al-hayula al-ula) in which the corporeal form (al-surah al-jismiyyah) inheres. For it was, before the advent of separation, qualified by one connection, being one connected thing, and after it, qualified by separation, rather by two connections that occur in it, as it then becomes two connected things. And the summary of it is that just as they proved, by the succession of different quantities upon the body while a substantial connective form (surah jawhariyyah ittisaliyyah) receptive to the successive quantities remains, that the changing quantum is other than the connection that remains unchanged, so too they proved, by the succession of different individual connections upon something that remains, that necessarily the changing connection is other than what corresponds to it. And they called the connection 'form' (surah), that which is receptive to it 'matter' (madah), and the composite of the two 'body' (jism).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a proof that body (jism) is composed of matter (hayula) and form (surah). The argument proceeds from the observation of change in connection and separation. When a connected body undergoes separation, its original connection ceases and it becomes two separate bodies. The author argues that there must be something that endures and is receptive to both connection and separation. That something cannot be the connection itself, because the connection ceases when separation occurs. The receding substance must be other than both connection and separation. This enduring substratum is what is called 'primary matter' (al-hayula al-ula), in which the corporeal form (al-surah al-jismiyyah) inheres. The author then provides a summary: just as variation in quantities while a substantial form remains proves the quantity is distinct from the form, similarly variation in connections proves the connection is distinct from the substratum. They term the connection 'form', the substratum 'matter', and their composite 'body'. The argument follows a logical structure: the fact that alternation occurs implies a permanent subject that is receptive to both states.

Key Terms
hayula (هيولى)
surah (صورة)
ittisal (اتصال)
infisal (انفصال)
jism (جسم)
madah (مادة)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the enduring substratum is itself a body or a physical substance.
Correction: The substratum is primary matter, which is not a body but a non-corporeal principle that underlies the body and receives form.

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason that the substratum of connection and separation cannot be the connection itself?
A) Because the connection is not permanent
B) Because the connection ceases when separation occurs, while the substratum endures
C) Because the connection is not a substance
D) Because the connection is an accident

Key Takeaway
The alternation of connection and separation in a body reveals a permanent matter (hayula) that is distinct from the changing form (surah), proving that every body is composed of matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a body's unity (connection) can change while its matter endures. Consider your own sense of identity across time. What is the unchanging 'matter' in you that allows you to remain 'you' despite the many changes you undergo? How does this affect your view of your soul?
?
This argument shows that what seems simple (a connected body) is actually composite. In your spiritual life, what do you assume is simple but might actually be composite, needing purification of form? How can you deepen your tawhid by recognizing the contingency of all created things?
?
Iji's proof relies on observation of a concrete phenomenon (connection ceasing) to infer an unseen substratum. When you look at the world around you, what other phenomena suggest a hidden reality? Are you content with surface-level understanding, or do you strive to uncover deeper truths that lead you to the Creator?

The Infinite Regress Objection Against Matter and Its Refutation
The author raises an objection of infinite regress for hayula and refutes it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of infinite regress against the proof of matter is raised and refuted by distinguishing matter's essential nature as pure potentiality.

Core Idea
Matter's receptivity does not require another matter because in itself it is pure potentiality.

This passage presents a critical objection to the proof of matter (hayula): if matter is receptive to connection and separation, then by the same logic used to prove matter for bodies, matter itself would need another matter, leading to an infinite regress. The response, drawing on earlier principles, clarifies that matter's receptivity is not an accidental property requiring a substratum; rather, matter in itself is pure potentiality (isti'dad) with no actual connection or separation. Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding a mistaken infinite regress in hylomorphic theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening part of the passage where the objector argues that matter must have another matter due to its receptivity to connection and separation.
Notice the logical parallel drawn to the proof of matter for bodies.
2
Identify the logical move
The objector claims that if receptivity to connection and separation proves matter in bodies, then matter itself must have a substratum, leading to infinite regress.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against the proponent of matter.
3
Read the response
The author refutes by distinguishing connection from the substratum that receives it. Matter's receptivity is not an accidental attribute requiring another matter.
Focus on the two conditions that would have to be met for the objection to hold.
4
Understand matter as pure potentiality
The response concludes that matter in itself is neither one nor many, neither connected nor separated—it is pure readiness (isti'dad). Its unity and multiplicity come from the forms that inhere in it.
This is the key insight that blocks the regress.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the refutation works: because matter's receptivity is not an accident separate from its essence; it is its very nature as potentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال في المعارضة لدليلهم الهيولى على تقدير وجودها إذا كانت واحدة كما قبل الانقسام كانت متصلة لا منفصل فيها، وإذا كانت كثيرة بورود الانقسام كانت منفصلة فهي قابلة للاتصال والانفصال، فلو اقتضى قبولهما إثبات هيولى كما ذكرتم في الجسم لزم أن يكون للهيول هيولى أخرى فتنقل الكلام إليها ويلزم التسلسل. وهو مندفع عنهم بما ذكرنا من التلخيص، فإنا أثبتنا كون الاتصال غير القابل للاتصال والانفصال المتعاقبين عليه فلا يلزم للهيولى هيولى أخرى إلا بإثبات أمرين أحدهما أن لها اتصالاً مغايراً لهذا الاتصال الذي هو حال فيها حتى متصلة تكون هي في حد ذاتها. والثاني : أنه أي ذلك الاتصال المغاير يزول عنها ويعود إليها حتى يثبت في ذات الهيولى شيئان : الاتصال المغاير وما هو قابل له فيكون للهيولى هيولى أخرى وذلك مما لا سبيل إليه. فإن وحدتها أي وحدة الهيولى وكثرتها بحسب ما يعرض لها من الاتصال ويقارنها من الصورة فهي قبل ورود الانفصال واحدة متصلة بالصورة الواحدة الحالة فيها وبعده متكثرة منفصلة بالصور المتعددة الحالة فيها، وإلا فهي في نفسها لا واحدة ولا كثيرة ولا متصلة ولا منفصلة إنما هي في ذاتها استعداد
And perhaps it is said in objection to their proof of matter (al-hayula): supposing it exists, it is one before division, so it is connected, having no separation in it. And when it becomes many by the occurrence of division, it becomes separated, so it is receptive to connection and separation. If their receptivity required the proof of a matter, as you mentioned for the body, then it would follow that matter has another matter, and the discussion would transfer to it, leading to infinite regress. This is refuted by what we have mentioned in the summary: we proved that connection is other than that which is receptive to the successive connection and separation. So it does not follow that matter has another matter except by establishing two things: one, that it has a connection that is other than this connection that inheres in it, so that it itself would be connected in its essence; and two, that this other connection ceases from it and returns to it, so that two things would be established in the essence of matter: the other connection and that which is receptive to it, and then matter would have another matter. That is not possible. For its unity, i.e., the unity of matter, and its multiplicity are according to what happens to it of connection and what accompanies it of form. So before the occurrence of separation, it is one, connected by the one form inhering in it; after it, it becomes multiple and separated by the multiple forms inhering in it. Otherwise, in itself, it is neither one nor many, neither connected nor separated; rather, in its essence, it is a readiness (isti'dad).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the proof of matter (hayula). The objection argues: if matter (hayula) is itself receptive to connection and separation, then by the same logic used to prove matter for bodies, it would follow that matter needs another matter to support that receptivity, leading to an infinite regress. The response, based on the earlier summary, distinguishes: connection is not the same as the substratum that receives it. The objection would only succeed if matter had its own intrinsic, separate connection distinct from the form that inheres in it. But that is not the case. Matter's unity and multiplicity are not essential to it but come from the forms and connections that inhere in it. In itself, matter is pure potentiality (isti'dad) with no actual connection or separation. Thus, no separate substratum is needed for matter itself, and the regress is avoided.

Key Terms
hayula (هيولى)
ittisal (اتصال)
infisal (انفصال)
tasalsul (تسلسل)
isti'dad (استعداد)
surah (صورة)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If matter is receptive to connection and separation, then it must itself be composed of a substratum and an accident, leading to an infinite regress of matters.
Correction: Matter's receptivity is not an accidental attribute; it is its very essence as potentiality. Matter in itself has no actual connection or separation, so it does not require a further substratum.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does matter (hayula) not require another matter to support its receptivity to connection and separation?
A) Because matter is eternal and unchanging
B) Because matter's connection is the same as the form that inheres in it
C) Because matter in itself is pure potentiality and has no actual connection or separation
D) Because matter is a body and does not change

Key Takeaway
Matter's essence is pure potentiality; its unity and multiplicity come from the forms that inhere in it, not from a separate substratum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you mistake your temporary states—your connections and separations, successes and failures—for your essence? How would your relationship with Allah change if you saw yourself as pure potentiality (isti'dad) before Him, shaped only by the 'form' of your obedience or disobedience?
?
The infinite regress objection warns against seeking an endless chain of underlying causes. In your own search for identity or purpose, do you rely on ever-changing external 'forms' (roles, possessions, relationships) rather than recognizing the simple, receptive core of your soul? What would it mean to turn to Allah from that core, stripped of all attachments?

Prime Matter's Unity: Mediated by Form
The commentator clarifies that prime matter in itself is neither one nor many, and explains the mediating role of form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prime matter in itself is neither one nor many and how form acts as an intermediary for these attributes.

Core Idea
Prime matter's unity is personal, not numerical; form bears unity and multiplicity.

This passage addresses a key question in natural philosophy: how can prime matter be both one and many? The commentator explains that in its own essence, prime matter is not characterized by unity or multiplicity; these come to it through form. Form acts as a conduit, receiving these attributes first and then passing them to matter. Understanding this distinction resolves apparent contradictions and prevents infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the first sentence of the passage and note that prime matter in itself is neither one nor many. This is the doctrine being defended.
The phrase 'stripped of unity and multiplicity' is key.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims this contradicts the Ash'ari position that the prime matter of elements is one in person despite multiple forms. Recognize the tension.
The example of a single piece of wood with different colors illustrates the point.
3
Grasp the answer's nuance
The answer distinguishes between personal unity (each matter has its own identity) and accidental unity/multiplicity (which come through form). Prime matter has personal unity.
This personal unity differentiates prime matter of elements from that of the heavens.
4
See form as intermediary
The commentator explains that attributes like unity, multiplicity, connection, and disconnection occur to form first and then to matter secondarily. Matter is not the direct subject.
There is no separate occurrence for matter; form is the true subject.
5
Apply the implications
Because form is the primary bearer, changes in form do not change matter's existence or individuation. This avoids an infinite regress of matter for matter.
Note the final clarification that 'act' here means actuality, not causal effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإلا فهي في نفسها لا (واحدة) أي وإن لا يعتبر معها الصورة الواحدة والمتعددة فهي في نفسها مسلوب عنها الوحدة والكثرة. فإن قلت : هذا مناف لما تقرر عندهم من أن هيولى العناصر مع تكثرها بحسب الصورة النوعية للبسائط العنصرية والمواليد الثلاثة واحدة بالشخص والصورة المذكورة الواردة عليها لا تصير لوحدتها الشخصية كخشب واحد لونت بعضها بألوان مختلفة. قلت : المراد أنه لا واحدة ولا متعددة بالوحدة والكثرة الحاصلتين من توارد الاتصالات ولها الوحدة الشخصية التي في نفسها بها يمتاز عن هيولى الأفلاك فهي ثابتة لها في حد ذاتها كسائر الموجودات الخارجية وتلك الوحدة تجامع الوحدة والكثرة المتواردتين عليها بحسب توارد الاتصاف. ومعناه أن الصورة تكون واسطة في عروض هذه الأحوال للهيولى بحيث تكون هذه الأحوال عارضة للصورة أولاً، وبالذات وللهيولى ثانياً، وبالعرض وليس لشيء من هذه الأحوال عروضان متغايران يكون أحدهما للهيولى والآخر للصورة، بل كان هناك عروض واحد يكون للصورة أولاً وللهيولي ثانياً على ما ذكرنا آنفاً. نعم كانت الهيولى واسطة في ثبوت هذه الأحوال للمحل الحقيقي الذي هو الصورة. ثم لا يذهب عليك أن الوحدة والاتصال هما لازمان لوجود الصورة قبل انقسام الجسم المتصل وأن الكثرة والاتصال هما لازمان لوجودها بعد انقسام الجسم المذكور، وأما الوجود والتشخص، فهما يكونان عارضين للهيولى بدون واسطة في العروض أصلاً وإن كان هناك واسطة في الثبوت أيضاً، فعلى هذا لا يلزم من تبدل وجودات الصور وتشخصاتها تبدل وجود الهيولى وتشخصها إذا المفروض أن الأحوال المذكورة أعني الوحدة وأخواتها لازمة لوجود الصورة وعارضة لها أولا وبالذات بخلاف الهيولى كما ذكرنا فحينئذ لا يلزم أن يكون للهيولى هيولى أخرى. هذا هو الكلام اللائق بما هو المختار عندهم وقوله : لا فعل لها الفعل هاهنا هو ما يقابل القوة لا ما هو بمعنى التأثير كما يتوهم.
He said: 'Otherwise, it (i.e., prime matter) in itself is not one' — meaning, even if the one and many form is not considered with it, then it in itself is stripped of unity and multiplicity. If you say: This contradicts what is established with them that the prime matter of the elements, despite its multiplicity according to the specific form of elemental simple bodies and the three generated beings, is one in person, and the mentioned form that comes upon it does not make it lose its personal unity, like a single piece of wood some of which are colored with different colors. I say: What is meant is that it is neither one nor many with the unity and multiplicity that result from the successive occurrences of attributes, and it has the personal unity by which it is distinguished from the prime matter of the heavens; that unity is established for it in its own essence like other external existents, and that unity coexists with the unity and multiplicity that come upon it according to successive attribution. And its meaning is that form is an intermediary in the occurrence of these states to prime matter, such that these states occur to form first and essentially, and to prime matter secondarily and accidentally. And there are not for any of these states two distinct occurrences, one for prime matter and another for form; rather there is one occurrence that is for form first and for prime matter second, as we mentioned earlier. Indeed, prime matter was an intermediary in the subsistence of these states for the true subject which is form. Then it should not escape you that unity and connection are necessary concomitants of the existence of form before the division of the continuous body, and that multiplicity and disconnection are necessary concomitants of its existence after the division of the mentioned body. As for existence and individuation, they occur to prime matter without any intermediary in occurrence at all, although there is an intermediary in subsistence as well. So according to this, from the change of existences and individuations of forms, there does not necessarily follow a change in the existence and individuation of prime matter, since it is supposed that the mentioned states — namely unity and its sisters — are necessary for the existence of form and occur to it first and essentially, contrary to prime matter as we mentioned. So then it does not necessarily follow that prime matter has another prime matter. This is the appropriate speech according to what is chosen by them. And his saying: 'It has no act' — act here is what is opposed to potentiality, not what is in the sense of effect as might be imagined.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification and a proof about the status of prime matter (hayula). It begins by stating that prime matter in itself is neither one nor many, because it only receives unity and multiplicity through form. Then an objection is raised: this seems to contradict the Ash'ari position that the prime matter of the four elements is one in person despite multiplication through forms. The answer distinguishes between two kinds of unity: the personal unity that belongs to prime matter in itself (like the heavens' hayula) and the unity and multiplicity that come to it through successive attributes. Then the commentator explains that form acts as an intermediary: attributes like unity, multiplicity, connection, and disconnection occur to form first and then to hayula secondarily. He further argues that existence and individuation occur directly to hayula, while unity and connection are necessary to form's existence before division. Thus changes in forms do not entail changes in hayula itself, so no infinite regress of hayula for hayula. Finally, he clarifies that 'act' (fi'l) here means actuality as opposed to potentiality, not causal effect.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prime matter is not one or many in any sense, ignoring the personal unity it possesses.
Correction: Prime matter has personal unity in its own essence, but it is neither numerically one nor many; unity and multiplicity come to it through form as an intermediary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does prime matter relate to unity and multiplicity?
A) It is one in itself
B) It is many in itself
C) It is neither one nor many in itself
D) It is both one and many in itself

Key Takeaway
Prime matter has personal unity but is not numerically one or many; these attributes are mediated by form, which bears them first and essentially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your own personal identity (like prime matter's personal unity) remains constant despite the many forms and changes you undergo. Consider a time when you felt your identity was threatened by a change in your circumstances or character. How does the truth that your core self is not defined by those passing attributes challenge you to cling to your unchanging worth before Allah?
?
The passage explains that form mediates attributes like unity and multiplicity to prime matter. In your life, what 'forms'—such as roles, possessions, or relationships—mediate your sense of unity and purpose? Are you mistaking these accidental forms for your essential self? How would your life change if you anchored your identity solely in your personal unity as a servant of Allah?
?
If prime matter's existence and individuation come directly without intermediary, then your own existence is direct from Allah. How does this realization confront the fear that your significance depends on your achievements or changes? What would it mean for you to live as if your existence is a constant gift, not a product of your fluctuating states?

Disagreement on Atomic Shapes and Sizes
Commentator notes disagreement on atomic shapes and sizes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that early theologians debated whether hypothetical indivisible atoms have varying shapes and sizes, and that the commentator only notes hesitation.

Core Idea
Atoms: same in constancy, but disputed in diversity.

This lesson addresses a subtle point in the physics of early kalām: even among those who accept indivisible atoms, there was uncertainty about whether those atoms are all identical in shape and size or come in varieties. The commentator highlights this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic phrase and its English translation. Notice the brevity—the commentator is glossing a prior claim without elaborating.
The passage is a commentary on a statement that atoms do not change shape or magnitude.
2
Identify the key term 'taraddud'
The word 'تردد' indicates hesitation or disagreement. This signals that the matter is not settled among scholars.
The commentator himself does not take a side; he merely reports the uncertainty.
3
Distinguish between constancy and diversity
Understand the logical point: the claim that atoms do not change (in shape or size) is separate from the question of whether atoms differ from each other. The passage notes that the latter is disputed.
This distinction is crucial for precision in theological physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لا تتغير أشكالها ومقاديرها أما تغاير أشكالها واختلافها كرية أو غير كرية أو اختلاف مقاديرها صغراً وكبراً ففيه تردد بينهم .
His statement: 'Their shapes and magnitudes do not change' — as for the variation of their shapes and their difference (whether spherical or non-spherical) or the difference of their magnitudes (smallness and largeness), there is hesitation among them concerning it.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This is a brief note on a point of disagreement among those who posit indivisible particles. The commentator observes that there is uncertainty (taraddud) about whether the shapes of these atoms vary (e.g., some might be spherical, others not) and whether their sizes can differ (some smaller, some larger). This passage is a gloss clarifying that the claim that atoms do not change in shape and magnitude does not settle the question of whether they differ from each other.

Correction: The passage only reports that there is disagreement (taraddud) on this point; it does not settle the matter.

Hyle as a Quality: Objection and Answer about Occupancy
Iji defines hyle as a quality in the body and answers an objection about occupancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that hyle (prime matter) is a quality that subsists in the bodily form dependently, and how the objection about its lack of independent occupancy is answered by distinguishing two modes of dependent occupancy.

Core Idea
Hyle is not a substance but a quality dependent on bodily form for occupancy.

Iji begins by refining the concept of hyle: it is not an independent substance but a quality that subsists in the bodily form. An objection arises: if hyle has no occupancy, how can bodily form, which occupies space, reside in it? The answer reveals a key distinction about dependent occupancy, central to understanding the composition of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
First read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument. Then read the commentary to clarify the technical terms.
Pay attention to the distinction between independent and dependent occupancy.
2
Identify the three parts
The passage has three parts: the definition (hyle as a quality), the objection (if no occupancy then bodily form cannot be characterized), and the answer (two kinds of dependent occupancy).
Mark where each part begins in the Arabic.
3
Understand the answer's logic
The answer rejects the objection by showing that dependent occupancy can be by inherence of another in the entity, not just the entity inhering in another. Hyle is the locus for bodily form, not an attribute of it.
This resolves the objection without making hyle a substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة على سبيل الاستقلال بل على سبيل التبعية للصورة الجسمية فالهيولي حينئذ صفة حالة في الجسمية تابعة لها في التحيز لا جوهر هو محل لها كما هو مطلوبكم وإلا فلا تختص الجسمية بها اختصاصاً ناعتاً لها لأنه أمر معقول محض لا تعلق ولا اختصاص له تعلق ولا اختصاص له بحيز قطعا فكيف يتصور حلول الجسمية المتحيزة بالذات فيه ؟ وقد يجاب بأنا لا نسلم أنها لو كانت متحيزة بالتبعية لكانت صفة للجسمية فإن تحيز الشيء بالتبعية قد يكون باعتبار حلوله في الغير، كما في الأعراض الحالة في الأجسام، وقد يكون باعتبار حلول الغير فيه، فليس يلزم من تحيز الهيولى لا بالاستقلال أن يكون تحيزها على سبيل حلولها في الجسمية بل يجوز يكون تحيزها بشرط حلول الجسمية فيها فتكون موصوفة بها لا صفة لها
The first observatory—the seventh inquiry: The body is composed of hyle and form, not independently but rather dependently on the bodily form. So hyle then is a quality subsisting in the body, dependent on it in occupancy, not a substance; it is a locus for it as you claim. Otherwise (if it had no occupancy either independently or dependently), then the bodily form would not be specifically characterized by it as a characterizing attribute, because that which has no occupancy at all is a pure intelligible, having no connection or specification with a space at all; so how can the bodily form, which is essentially occupying, be imagined to inhere in it? It may be answered: We do not accept that if it were occupying dependently it would be an attribute of the bodily form, for the occupancy of a thing dependently may be by way of its inherence in another, as in accidents subsisting in bodies, or by way of the inherence of another in it. So it does not follow from hyle's not having independent occupancy that its occupancy is by way of its inhering in the bodily form; rather, it is possible that its occupancy is conditional upon the bodily form's inhering in it, so it is qualified by it, not an attribute of it.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definition and objection-answer. It begins by stating that hyle (prime matter) is not a substance but a quality (ṣifa) subsisting in the bodily form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya), dependent on it for occupancy (taḥayyuz). The objection then raises: if hyle has no independent occupancy, then the bodily form cannot be specifically characterized by it, because hyle would be a pure mental concept with no spatial connection. The answer distinguishes two ways of dependent occupancy: either the dependent entity inheres in another (like an accident in a body), or the other inheres in it. Hyle's occupancy is of the second kind: the bodily form inheres in hyle, so hyle is qualified by the form, not the other way around.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة جسمية
تحيز
تبعية
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that hyle must be a substance to be a locus for body.
Correction: The passage clarifies hyle can be a quality that provides a locus through dependent occupancy where the form inheres in it.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is hyle's relationship to the bodily form?
A) Hyle is a substance in which the bodily form inheres.
B) Hyle is a quality that inheres in the bodily form.
C) Hyle is an attribute of the bodily form.
D) Hyle is independent of the bodily form in occupancy.

Key Takeaway
Hyle is a quality that provides a locus for bodily form through a mode of dependent occupancy where the form inheres in hyle, not vice versa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are not a substance independent of Allah; your very existence is contingent. How does the idea of being a quality dependent on a form resonate with your own sense of self? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient? This lesson forces you to acknowledge that all occupancy is derivative. Write down one area in your life where you have been acting as if you are the substance rather than the quality. What would change if you truly accepted your dependent status?
?
The passage distinguishes between two modes of dependence. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes think your deeds inhere in you as if you are the subject, rather than you inhering in His mercy? Reflect on a specific sin or good deed: are you treating it as if it defines you independently, or as something that only exists because of His grace? Commit to reorienting one matter today.

Conceiving Body and Its Parts: An Objection and Answer
Iji presents and refutes the argument that conceiving body entails conceiving its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the composition of body from hyle and form, and how Iji's answer defends it by distinguishing between ordinary conception and essential conception.

Core Idea
Our failure to conceive hyle does not disprove its existence.

In this passage, Iji addresses a powerful objection against the doctrine that body is composed of hyle and form. The objector argues that if body had such parts, simply thinking of body would force us to think of those parts. Iji's response marks a crucial distinction between conceiving something's external form and grasping its inner reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'لزم من تعقله تعقلهما') to understand the logical conditional being proposed.
Note the structure: 'if... then... but...'
2
Identify the objector's conclusion
See how the objector claims that because we do not conceive hyle when conceiving body, the premise of composition must be false.
The objector uses our ordinary conceptual experience as evidence.
3
Analyze Iji's answer
Read the answer (منع تعقل حقيقته) and understand how Iji denies that we conceive the true essence of body. This blocks the conclusion.
The key term is 'كنه' (essence/reality).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في نفي الهيولى وإبطال تركيب الجسم منها لو كان الجسم مركباً من جزأين كما ذكرتم لزم من تعقله تعقلهما ولم يحتج في ثبوت شيء منهما له إلى برهان واللازم باطل فإنا نعقل الجسم ولا نعقل الهيولى ونحتاج في إثباتها إلى البرهان والجواب منع تعقل حقيقته يعني أن ما ذكرتم إنما يلزم إذا كان حقيقة الجسم معقولة بالكنه وهو ممنوع
It may be said in denying hyle and invalidating the composition of body from it: If the body were composed of two parts as you mentioned, then from conceiving it would follow conceiving them, and there would be no need for a proof for the existence of either of them. But the consequent is false, for we conceive the body and do not conceive hyle, and we need a proof to establish it. The answer: We deny that its essence is conceived (by the mind), meaning that what you mentioned would only follow if the reality of the body were conceived in its essence (bi'l-kunh), which is denied.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objection argues: if body is composed of hyle and form, then simply conceiving body should give us conception of its parts (hyle and form) without proof. But we conceive body without conceiving hyle, and we need a proof for hyle, so the premise of composition is false. The answer denies the premise: we do not conceive the essence (ḥaqīqa) of body in its ultimate reality (bi'l-kunh); we only conceive its external aspect. Therefore, our failure to conceive hyle does not disprove its existence.

Key Terms

حقيقة
كنه
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Our ordinary conception of a thing reveals its true essence and composition.
Correction: Iji shows that we only conceive external aspects; the inner reality may require proof.

Checkpoint
What is the objection's main claim?
A) Hyle cannot exist because it is not perceived.
B) If body were composed of hyle and form, we would conceive both parts when conceiving body.
C) The proof for hyle is invalid.
D) Body is simple and has no parts.

Key Takeaway
The objection that conceiving body should reveal its parts is refuted by noting that we do not conceive the essence of body in its ultimate reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth of Islam—like the reality of the angels, the soul, or the attributes of Allah—simply because you could not fully conceive or grasp it with your mind? What does this lesson demand that you change in that attitude?
?
The objection assumes that if we cannot directly conceive something, we should deny it. In your own life, where do you tend to rely only on what you can clearly perceive, ignoring the deeper realities that require humble submission to revelation? Commit today to one unseen truth you will actively affirm.
?
The answer humbly admits that we do not conceive even physical things in their ultimate essence. Does your heart find peace in that limitation, or does it resist? What would it mean for you to accept that your knowledge of everyday things is incomplete—and then to extend that humility to your knowledge of the Divine?

Ibn Sina's Proof of Hyle for All Bodies
Ibn Sina argues from the unity of extension's nature to prove hyle in all bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina proves that even celestial bodies, which do not actually separate, must have hyle (matter) based on the unity of the nature of extension and the diversity of accidental properties.

Core Idea
The oneness of extension's nature demands a common substrate for all bodies.

Ibn Sina acknowledges that the standard proof for hyle (the 'method of separation') only works for bodies that can actually be divided, like the elements. Celestial bodies, which indivisible on Aristotle's view, seem to escape this proof. To cover them, Ibn Sina offers a deeper argument: the nature of extension is one specific nature for all bodies, and differences arise only from external accidents, implying a shared substrate that receives those accidents. In this lesson, we will walk through this elegant argument.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the limitation of the standard proof
The method of separation (maslak al-infiṣāl) only proves hyle for bodies that can actually connect and separate, like elemental bodies. It doesn't cover celestial bodies.
This sets up the need for a new proof.
2
Understand the premise: extension's nature is one specific nature
Ibn Sina states that the nature of extension (the bodily form extended in itself) is a single specific nature for all bodies. This means that body-ness as such is uniform.
Differences between bodies are not due to differences in extension itself.
3
See how differences come from external accidents
If one body differs from another (hot/cold, elemental/celestial), those differences come from outside the nature of extension. They are accidental, not essential.
Accidental properties require a subject that receives them.
4
Contrast with quantity to clarify 'specific nature'
Quantity (like line or surface) is a genus that becomes specific through essential differentiate; the nature of extension is already specific but receives accidental differentiate.
This reinforces that extension's nature is uniform at the specific level.
5
Conclude that all bodies need hyle
Since the nature of extension is one specific nature, and its requirements do not differ across individuals, if one body needs hyle (as proven by the method of separation), then all bodies, including celestial ones, need it.
Therefore hyle exists in every body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم في تفريعات لهم على وجود الهيولى أحدها إثبات الهيولى لكل جسم وإنما احتيج إلى هذا الإثبات إذ تلك الحجة التي هي المعول عليها في إثباتها أعني مسلك الانفصال كما عرفت لا تثبتها إلا لما يقبل الاتصال والانفصال بالفعل كالعنصريات ولعل بعض الأجسام لا يقبلهما كالفلكيات على رأيهم فلا بد لإثبات الهيولى فيها من بيان آخر فقال ابن سينا طبيعة الاتصال أي الصورة الجسمية المتصلة في نفسها للجميع أي لجميع الأجسام طبيعة واحدة نوعية لأن جسمية إذا خالفت جسمية أخرى كان ذلك لأجل أن هذه حارة وتلك باردة، أو هذه لها طبيعة عنصرية وتلك لها طبيعة فلكية، إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي تلحق الجسمية من خارج فإن الجسمية أمر موجود في الخارج، والطبيعة الفلكية مثلاً موجود آخر قد انضاف هذه الطبيعة في الخارج إلى الطبيعة الجسمية الممتازة عنها في الوجود بخلاف المقدار، فإنه أمر مبهم لا يوجد في الخارج ما لم يتنوع بفصول ذاتية بأن يكون خطاً أو سطحاً مثلاً، وكل ما كان اختلافه بالخارجات دون الفصول، كان طبيعة نوعية ومقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يختلف فإذا ثبت احتياجه
The first observatory—the eighth inquiry: Establishing hyle in the body. Among their corollaries on the existence of hyle, one is establishing hyle for every body. This proof is needed because the argument relied upon to establish it—namely the method of separation (maslak al-infiṣāl) as you know—only establishes it for what actually accepts connection and separation, like the elements; and perhaps some bodies do not accept them, like the celestial bodies in their view. So another demonstration is needed to establish hyle in them. Ibn Sina said: The nature of extension, i.e., the bodily form extended in itself, is one specific nature for all (bodies). Because if one body differs from another, that is only because this one is hot and that one is cold, or this one has an elemental nature and that one has a celestial nature, and so on, among the matters that attach to body from outside. For body-ness is something existing in the external world, and the celestial nature, for example, is another existent that has been added externally to the body-ness that is distinct from it in existence. Unlike quantity (al-miqdār), which is a vague thing that does not exist in the external world unless it is diversified by essential differentiate, such as being a line or a surface. And everything whose difference is by external (accidents) rather than (essential) differentiate is a specific nature; and the requirement of a specific nature does not differ. So if its need is established...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a proof. It begins by noting that the standard proof for hyle (the method of separation, based on actual division) only works for bodies that can be actually divided, like elements. For celestial bodies, which are indivisible on Aristotle's view, another proof is needed. Ibn Sina supplies this proof: the nature of extension (ṭabīʿat al-ittiṣāl) is one specific (nawʿiyya) nature for all bodies. Differences between bodies come from external accidents (like heat, cold, elemental vs. celestial nature). Since these accidents attach from outside, the body must have a substrate (hyle) that receives them. The passage contrasts this with quantity (miqdār), which is a genus that only becomes specific through essential differentiate; body's nature, however, is already specific but receives accidental differentiate. Because the requirement of a specific nature does not differ across its individuals, if one body needs a substrate, all do. The passage cuts off mid-sentence but the argument is that so the need is established.

عنصريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies do not need hyle because they are indivisible and do not undergo change.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that even if they do not actually separate, their accidental properties (like being celestial) require a substrate. The unity of extension's nature implies that all bodies, regardless of divisibility, need hyle.

Checkpoint

A) Celestial bodies have no extension.

C) Celestial bodies are composed of mind only.
D) The method of separation is invalid for all bodies.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina argues that the unity of the nature of extension across all bodies, with differences from external accidents, necessitates a common substrate (hyle) for all bodies, including celestial ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all bodies, from the dust beneath your feet to the farthest stars, share the same need for a substrate, does that not humble the pride you take in your own physical form? How will you remind yourself daily that your body is not self-sufficient but utterly dependent on its Creator?
?
Ibn Sina proved that even the heavens are not exempt from needing a substrate. In what areas of your life do you subconsciously assume you are self-sufficient and independent of Allah? What concrete step will you take today to break that assumption?
?
The nature of extension is one across all bodies, and differences are only accidental. Think of someone you dislike or look down upon. Is your dislike based on accidental qualities (appearance, status, ethnicity) that are external to their essential humanity? Will you deliberately seek to see the shared nature Allah has given you both, and treat them accordingly starting this week?

Ibn Sina's Argument for Matter in All Bodies
Ibn Sina argues that the body-form is one specific nature necessitating matter universally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's reasoning that the bodily form is a single specific nature, which necessitates the presence of matter in all bodies, including celestial ones.

Core Idea
A single specific nature cannot have different requirements; if it needs matter in one body, it needs it in all.

Ibn Sina presents a powerful argument to show that every body, whether elemental or celestial, must contain matter. He begins by analyzing the nature of the bodily form (the form of extension) and argues that it is a single specific nature, not varying in itself. From this, he draws a universal conclusion about its dependence on matter. This passage is a classic example of philosophical reasoning in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the contrast between body-form and quantity.
2
Identify the key claim about body-form
Notice that Ibn Sina claims body-form is a single specific nature. Find the reason he gives: differences come from external factors, not from the form itself.
He contrasts this with quantity, which requires intrinsic differentiate.
3
Understand the logical implication
If body-form needs matter in elemental bodies (where it inheres), then because it is a single nature, it must need matter in all bodies. Otherwise, it would have contradictory properties.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the body-form cannot subsist by itself in any body; it always requires matter. The passage ends by emphasizing that a single reality cannot have differing concomitants.
This argument is meant to apply universally, including to celestial spheres.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال ابن سينا طبيعة الاتصال ) أي الصورة الجسمية المتصلة في نفسها ( للجميع ) أي لجميع الأجسام طبيعة ( واحدة ) نوعية لأن جسمية إذا خالفت جسمية أخرى كان ذلك لأجل أن هذه حارة وتلك باردة، أو هذه لها طبيعة عنصرية وتلك لها طبيعة فلكية، إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي تلحق الجسمية من خارج فإن الجسمية أمر موجود في الخارج، والطبيعة الفلكية مثلاً موجود آخر قد انضاف هذه الطبيعة في الخارج إلى الطبيعة الجسمية الممتازة عنها في الوجود بخلاف المقدار، فإنه أمر مبهم لا يوجد في الخارج ما لم يتنوع بفصول ذاتية بأن يكون خطاً أو سطحاً مثلاً، وكل ما كان اختلافه بالخارجات دون الفصول، كان طبيعة نوعية ومقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يختلف ) فإذا ثبت احتياجه ) أي احتياج الاتصال الذي هو الصورة الجسمية ( إلى المادة ) في الأجسام العنصرية لكونه حالاً فيها ( امتنع قيامه بنفسه في شيء من الأجسام ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع قيامه بنفسه بل قام بذاته في الفلك مثلاً ( كان ) ذلك الاتصال الجوهري (في حد ذاته غنياً عن المحل والغنى عن المحل لا يحل فيه ) أصلاً . ( وبالجملة فالحقيقة الواحدة ) النوعية لا تختلف (لوازمها) ومقتضياتها ( فتكون ( بالنصب على أنه جواب النفي (قائمة بذاتها تارة، وبالغير أخرى كما لا تكون جوهراً مرة وعرضاً أخرى ) أي كما أن انقلاب الحقائق محال كذلك اختلاف لوازم حقيقة واحدة محال لاستلزامه أن لا تكون تلك الحقيقة تلك الحقيقة بل حقيقة أخرى )
Ibn Sina said: The nature of connection, i.e., the bodily form connected in itself, for all (bodies) is one specific nature, because if one body-form differs from another body-form, that is only because this one is hot and that one is cold, or this one has an elemental nature and that one has a celestial nature, and other such things that attach to body-form from outside. For body-form is something existing in the external world, and the celestial nature, for example, is another existence that has been added externally to the bodily nature which is distinct from it in existence, unlike quantity, for it is something ambiguous that does not exist externally unless it is diversified by essential differentiate, such that it is a line or a surface, etc. And everything whose difference is due to external things rather than differentiate is a specific nature, and the requirement of a specific nature does not differ. So when it is established that connection, i.e., the bodily form, needs matter in elemental bodies because it inheres in them, it is impossible for it to subsist by itself in any body. Otherwise, i.e., if it did not subsist by itself but subsisted in itself in the celestial sphere, for example, then that substantial connection would in itself be independent of a locus, and that which is independent of a locus does not inhere in it at all. In summary, a single specific nature does not have differing concomitants and requirements, so it would [have to] subsist by itself sometimes and by another sometimes, just as a substance cannot be once a substance and once an accident. That is, just as the transformation of realities is impossible, likewise the difference in the concomitants of a single reality is impossible, because it would require that that reality not be that reality but another reality.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's argument for the necessity of matter in all bodies, including celestial ones. The argument proceeds from the claim that the bodily form (the nature of extension) is a single specific nature. Ibn Sina argues that if body-form differed, it would be due to external factors like heat/cold or elemental/celestial nature, not from within. This is contrasted with quantity, which is ambiguous and requires intrinsic differentiate to exist. Since body-form is a specific nature, its requirements are uniform. If it requires matter in some bodies (elemental), it must require it in all. The conclusion is that the bodily form cannot subsist by itself in any body; otherwise it would be independent of matter, but its nature as a specific nature precludes such variation. The passage ends by emphasizing that a single reality cannot have differing concomitants.

The Existence of Hyle and the Refutation of Three Possibilities
Sanusi classifies and refutes three possibilities for form entering hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for the existence of hyle in bodies and why only one of the three possible ways of form entering hyle is viable, requiring a specifier for location.

Core Idea
Hyle must be embodied, and only a specifier can prevent it from being in all places or nowhere.

Sanusi now tackles a central problem in physics: how does prime matter (hyle) receive form? He first establishes that every body requires a space, then considers three logical possibilities for how corporeal form enters hyle, and refutes all but one, which in turn requires a specifier to explain why a given body is in a particular place. This passage is dense but pivotal for understanding kalam cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: a premise about bodies and spaces, then three possibilities, then refutations and an objection.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Find the three possibilities for how corporeal form relates to hyle: (1) it exists in all spaces simultaneously, (2) it exists in no space, (3) it exists in some spaces but not others. Which ones are dismissed and why?
The first two are dismissed because a body cannot be in two places at once, and form must have a space.
3
Understand the role of the specifier
The third possibility requires a specifier to pick out a particular space. Grasp why the specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) is insufficient—it only specifies a universal place, not a specific part of that place.
This is the key subtlety: the specific form has a uniform relation to all parts of the universal space.
4
Follow the objection and answer
Consider the objection about the part of earth: it seems to have a specifier without an infinite regress. See how Sanusi answers by tracing the specification back to prior forms and positions, leading to an infinite chain.
This answer defends the necessity of a specifier and shows that the apparent counterexample actually supports the argument.

The First Observatory – The Eighth Objective: Proving the Hyle in Body. If there is an indication to it (the hyle), then it is either a body or a thing inhering in a body, because the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is impossible. That is because if it has position, i.e., is subject to sensory indication, then if it divides in directions, it is a body, i.e., a corporeal form, because it is the body at first glance as passed; if it does not divide at all, it is an indivisible atom; if it divides in one direction or in two directions only, it is a line or surface — not substantial (jūhārī) because they are in the ruling of the indivisible atom as you have learned, but accidental — so the hyle then becomes a thing inhering in the body, not a locus for the corporeal form, which is a reductio. Otherwise, i.e., if there is no indication to it, meaning it is not extended (mutaḥayyiz), neither essentially nor by indirection, and there is no doubt that it is receptive to the corporeal form, since the discussion is about the hyle of bodies. His statement: (That is etc.) The clearer and more concise is to say: if it is pointed to independently, it is a body, i.e., a substance with volume; if by indirection, it is a state in a body, whether it be a point, line, surface, instructional body, or other, because the indivisible atom and what is in its ruling are impossible. So it is not an indivisible atom, nor a line, nor a surface, nor a state in one of them — this on the assumption of disregarding its substantially. Therefore it is necessary to suffice with its being a body. As for what the commentator (al-shārif) mentioned, it contains disturbance, because if he restricts sensory indication to independence, his statement 'not substantial but accidental' is not valid; and if he does not restrict it, his statement 'it is a body, i.e., a corporeal form' is not valid because it could be an instructional body. His statement: (But accidental) — apparently it is an apostrophe from his statement 'substantial', i.e., rather a line or surface accidentally. But there is the possibility that it is a point. So one must mention it, unless it is said: rather, an accidental thing, which includes the point as well. But here, apostrophe from false to false is meaningless. The correct is to say: and if it does not divide at all, or divides in one direction or two, it is a point or surface because of the impossibility of the indivisible atom and what is in its ruling. His statement: (Then the hyle becomes a thing inhering etc.) i.e., a corporeal form; he alerts because that is necessary from its being divided in three directions, not from its being composed of hyle and form. His statement: (And the manifestations) — they are the particularities of species, kinds, and individuals. His statement: (For lack of a specifier) — and soon the objection against it will come in his statement: And it may be said: it is possible that a form accompanies the hyle, any personal form for example. The First Observatory – The Eighth Objective: Proving the Hyle in Body. Body, and every body must have a space, and it is impossible for one body to be in two places or more at one time. The last (division) is false for lack of a specifier (lā makhkhas) with respect to that part, because the hyle on that estimation has a uniform relation to all spaces, and likewise the relation of the corporeal form — it requires an absolute space, not a specific one. For it is said: Perhaps a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) inheres in the hyle along with the inherence of the corporeal form, and it specifies it to a specific space. Also, what you have mentioned is refuted by the specific part of earth and the rest of the universal elements — its specification to its specific space without a specifier that necessitates it, for the relation of the parts of the universal element to the parts of its space is uniform, yet each one of its parts is obtained in a specific space. We say: The specific form, although it determines a universal place, yet its relation to all parts of the space of the whole is one, so the discussion is about its specification to its specific space from the parts of the space of the whole. For the embodied hyle with that specific form either obtains in each one of those parts, or in some of them, or does not obtain in any of them — all are false. And it may be said: It is possible that another form or a state from among the states accompanies the hyle, specifying for it some parts of the universal place. Also, the abstracted hyle may be the hyle of a universal element, so there is no need for specification beyond the specific form. If you say: We transfer the discussion to the specification of the parts of that element to their partial places, we say: Those parts are assumed in it, not existing in the external, so they do not require a place. Also, it is possible to assume there a state specifying the parts with a particular position. And the part of earth is only specified to its specific space which it is in because its matter before that earthly form had another form specifying that part to that space, or specifying it to another space from which that part moved by straight line to that space. The upshot is that the specifier of that part of earth to its specific space is the prior position obtained for its matter due to a prior form either in that space or in another space from which that part after acquiring its earthly form moved to its space by the shortest path. And that prior form is preceded by a third form, and so on to infinity. His statement: (in two places) — the clearer is 'in two spaces'. His statement: (Perhaps a form etc.) — it is answered by transferring the discussion to the particularity of that specific form. His statement: (as for that [space] is like a part of air, and air left its natural space and occurred in...) His statement: (For if you say) is an answer to his statement: 'so there is no need in specification etc.' and his statement: 'Also, it is possible etc.' is a completion of his statement: 'We say: those parts.' His statement: (by the shortest path, like the straight line for example.)
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof and refutation regarding the existence of hyle (prime matter) in physical bodies. It begins by defining the nature of hyle: if it can be pointed to (i.e., has position), it must be either a body (if divisible in three dimensions) or a state inhering in a body (if it is a point, line, or surface), because the indivisible atom is impossible. If it cannot be pointed to (i.e., is not extended), then it is receptive to corporeal form, since the discussion concerns the hyle of bodies. The commentary then refines this: the hyle, if independently pointed to, is a body; if by indirection, it is a state in a body (point, line, surface, or instructional body). The commentator criticizes a previous view for inconsistency. Next, the passage classifies three possibilities when corporeal form enters hyle: either the form exists with it in all spaces (places), or in none, or in some. The first two are necessarily false because a single body cannot be in multiple places simultaneously, and form cannot exist without space. The third is false due to lack of a specifier (makhkhas), since hyle and corporeal form have uniform relation to all spaces. An objection is then raised: perhaps a specific form (like a species form) determines a particular place, and also the example of a specific part of earth seems to have a specifier. The answer is that the specific form determines only a universal place, not a particular part of that place, and the part of earth's specification is traced back to prior forms and positions, leading to an infinite regress. The commentary further clarifies the objection and answer, emphasizing that the parts of a universal element are merely assumed, not externally existent, and that a state or prior form can serve as specifier. The overall argument defends the necessity of a specifier for hyle's embodiment and refutes alternative explanations.

Key Terms
الجسم
الحيز
الصورة الجسمية
المخصص
الصورة النوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) is sufficient to specify a particular space (e.g., this patch of earth specifically).
Correction: The specific form only determines a universal place (e.g., the earth as a whole), not a particular part of it. Its relation to all parts of the universal space is uniform, so it cannot pick out one part without an additional specifier.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the third possibility (hyle with corporeal form in some spaces but not others) false without a specifier?
A) Because hyle cannot extend in space.
B) Because the relation of hyle and corporeal form to all spaces is uniform, so there is no reason to prefer one space over another.
C) Because corporeal form requires a specific space, not an absolute one.
D) Because bodies cannot move between spaces.

Key Takeaway
The only way to explain why a body is in a specific place is through a specifier, ultimately traced back to an infinite chain of prior forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that without a specifier, a body would have no reason to be in one place rather than another. Reflect on one specific aspect of your life—your current location, your health, your abilities. Can you honestly attribute that particular 'specification' to mere chance or your own doing? If not, what does this force you to acknowledge about the One who has specified your existence in this exact state?
?
Sanusi shows that even the particular piece of earth's position is determined by an infinite chain of prior forms. Consider your own position in life—your family, your circumstances, your faith. Are you willing to accept that your entire situation is the result of a chain of causes that ultimately points to a single Specifier? How does this change your sense of control and your trust in divine wisdom?
?
If every body's specific location requires a specifier, then nothing in the universe is accidental. Think of one event in your past that you considered random or insignificant. In light of this lesson, can you still view it as without purpose? Commit to seeing that event as deliberately placed by the Specifier, and ask yourself: What might He be teaching you through that specific placement?

Every Body Has a Natural Shape: Proof by Necessary Finitude
Defines body types and proves every body has a natural shape via finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every body—whether simple or composite—must have a natural shape, based on the necessity of its finitude, and how shape is a concomitant of existence not essence.

Core Idea
Finitude entails a boundary; that boundary is the natural shape of every body.

This passage lays the foundation for understanding the physical structure of bodies. The author defines simple and composite bodies, then asserts a universal rule: every body has a natural shape. The proof relies on the body's necessary finitude, which implies a boundary. However, this raises questions about whether shape belongs to the essence or existence of a body, and whether simple bodies really require finitude. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Define body types
Read the definitions of simple and composite bodies. A simple body is not composed of bodies of different natures; a composite is the opposite.
These definitions are specific to this physical context, not the usual logical ones.
2
Grasp the universal claim
The author states that every body, whether simple or composite, has a natural shape. Note that this is presented as a general rule (hukm 'amm).
3
Understand the proof
The proof: every body must be finite (infinite dimensions are impossible). Finitude means a boundary, which is the shape. So shape follows necessarily from finitude.
The author mentions that shape is a concomitant of existence, not essence – this anticipates an objection.
4
Consider the objection
An objector argues that simple bodies do not necessitate finitude; only composites do. The passage then offers a response distinguishing shape as a concomitant of existence.
This deepens the understanding: shape is not inherent in the quiddity but arises when the body exists.

His saying: (For every body a natural shape, and that due to the necessity of its finitude.) Every body either necessitates the finitude of its dimensions or necessitates the non-finitude of its dimensions, or necessitates something of them, and the accepted of these divisions is the third, and the first two are denied for simple bodies, so it does not follow that they have natural shapes as they claimed. As for composites, it is apparent that they necessitate finitude and shaping, as in the limbs of animals and the branches of trees, based on the doctrine of determinism, not of free choice. And the commentator objected in his gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid and said: It is objected to him that its shape depends on the finitude of dimensions, and there is no doubt that the nature of the body does not necessitate the finitude of its dimensions, and what occurs to a thing by means not based on itself is not an accident for it by itself. If you say: This very thing applies to place as well, because its occurrence in it depends on the existence of place, which is not based on the essence of the body. I say: The existence of a body is not conceivable in a non-place according to the one who says it is dimension; so the existence of place is among the concomitants of its existence insofar as it is, unlike the finitude of dimensions, for it is not among the concomitants of the existence of the body insofar as it is. Because the intermediary, if it is not based on the essence of the thing and is not necessary for it, is certainly something extraneous, unlike what is based on its essence or what necessarily follows from its essence insofar as it is. Yes, there is no doubt in its applicability on the doctrine that place is the surface, for it is not necessary for the existence of the body, as in the finite, but depends on the existence of another body, and that is something extraneous. Up to here is his speech, and it is possible to answer some of it, so reflect.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage opens with definitions and a general proof. First, it defines simple and composite bodies in the context of natural composition: a simple body's reality is not compounded from bodies of different natures, while a composite is the opposite. Then it states the main rule: every body, whether simple or composite, has a natural shape. The proof given is that every body must be finite (because infinite dimensions are impossible), and finiteness entails a boundary, which is a shape. The author then clarifies that shape is a concomitant of existence, not of essence—this refutes an objection that shape would be necessary for the body's quiddity. The passage then presents a further objection: the proof does not apply to simple bodies because they do not necessarily imply finitude. The objector argues that only composites obviously demand finitude and shape. The commentator responds by distinguishing shape as a property of existence, not essence, and argues that the existence of a body always entails finitude, thus shape. Finally, a discussion about place (al-makan) is brought: if place is considered dimension, it is a necessary concomitant of body, but if place is considered surface, it is not necessary and depends on another body. The passage ends with the suggestion that these difficulties can be answered, inviting reflection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That shape is a necessary property of the body's essence (quiddity) itself, such that an infinite body would still have a shape.
Correction: Shape is a concomitant of existence, not essence. An infinite body is impossible, but even if it were possible, it would not have a shape because shape depends on finitude. The proof relies on existence necessitating finitude.

Key Takeaway
Every body, whether simple or composite, must have a natural shape because its existence necessitates finitude, and finitude implies a boundary, which is the shape—this shape is a concomitant of existence, not essence.

The passage says shape is a concomitant of existence, not essence. Consider something you cling to—a role, a possession, a relationship—as if it defines who you are. Is it truly essential to your identity, or is it merely a temporary concomitant of your existence? What would it cost you to release it?
?
If every finite body has a natural shape, then your finitude is not a flaw but a sign of being created. How does knowing that your very existence is a gift—not a given—change your attitude toward the limits of your life, your time, and your abilities? What will you start doing differently today to honor that gift?

The Proof of Spherical Shape
Proof that the natural shape of a simple body is a sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to classical Islamic theologians, the natural shape of a simple body must be a sphere based on the principle of one power acting on one matter.

Core Idea
A single nature in a single matter can only produce one kind of action—only the sphere satisfies this.

In this passage, the theologian proves that a simple body—one with a single nature and a single matter—cannot have a polygonal or any other shape. He argues that a single power acting on a single matter can only produce one type of action. Since polygonal shapes involve different types of surfaces and angles, they require multiple actions, which is impossible for a simple body. Only the sphere, with its single continuous surface, fits this requirement.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the key terms: قوة واحدة, مادة واحدة, مضلع.
2
Understand the premise
The argument begins with the fact that a simple body has one power (nature) and one matter. Why must a single power produce a single action?
The premise is considered self-evident.
3
See the application to shapes
The author contrasts the sphere with the polygon. In a polygon, different sides involve different actions (line, angle, surface). This would require multiple actions from one power, which is impossible.
4
Conclude the natural shape
Therefore, the only natural shape for a simple body is the sphere. The author also mentions that composite bodies have varying shapes, so he does not discuss them.
