Correction of the Thickness of Complementary Spheres
Correction of thickness relation in complementary spheres

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the thickness of complementary spheres is double the eccentricity, not equal, and how demonstration and textual interpretation support this correction.

Core Idea
The thickness of complementary spheres is twice the eccentricity, not equal.

In this passage, the commentator corrects a common mistake regarding the complementary spheres. While the author of the Mawāqif stated that thickness equals eccentricity, the commentator argues from both demonstration and geometry that it is actually double. This correction is essential for understanding the structure of the spheres and the precision of astronomical models.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main correction
Read the passage and locate the specific judgment being corrected. Note the phrase 'sawa al-hukm bi-anna ghulz kull minhuma yusawi miqdar kharuj al-markaz' and its correction.
The correction is: thickness equals double the eccentricity.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'amount'
The commentator explains that the objection only applies if 'amount' (al-hayz) means the actual distance; if it means the verbal sense (the act of exiting), then the amount is double. Grasp this distinction.
This saves the author's wording from inconsistency.
3
Follow the geometric proof
The commentator provides a demonstration using points A, B, C on the convexity. Visualize how the volume of the sphere relates to the eccentricity.
Refer to the diagram mentioned as 'al-ashkal al-marsuma' for clarity.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The commentator offers an alternative reading of the author's phrase: 'bi-qadr kharuj al-markaz' means 'according to' not 'equal to'. Understand how this reconciles the statement.
This shows the commentator's effort to harmonize with the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The First Observation - Second Chapter - First Section - Third Goal: On the Sphere of the Fixed Stars. All the judgments related to the complementary spheres are correct except the judgment that the thickness of each of them equals the amount of the eccentricity. Indeed, the correct is that the thickness of each is double that amount, as proven by demonstration, and sound imagination bears witness to it for even the least intelligent. His saying: '(Indeed, the correct, etc.)' This only applies if by 'amount' is meant what comes out. But if it is meant in the verbal sense, then the meaning is an amount obtained by the eccentricity of its center from the center of the world, which is double the distance between the two centers, so it is not as is hidden. His saying: '(As proven by demonstration)' Its explanation: If we suppose that A B C is the convexity of a sphere, then the exterior in its interior and its concavity, and from and to A or from to B and from D to C, that is the volume of that sphere. His saying: '(Except the judgment that the thickness of each of them, etc.)' It can be said in correction of the author's statement that the meaning of his saying 'by the amount of the eccentricity' is 'according to the eccentricity,' and that one is related to the other, not that the thickness of each of the complements equals the amount of the eccentricity such that the commentator's objection applies. In summary, the thickness of the complement is related to the eccentricity; if that amount is, for example, the thickness of the complement is twice the diameter of the earth. Thus, the thickness of the complement is double the amount of the eccentricity. This appears in the figures drawn in perceptibles for depicting the eccentric spheres. Refer to them, and more explanation will come if God wills.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a correction of a judgment regarding the complementary spheres. The author had stated that the thickness of each complementary sphere equals the amount of eccentricity (kharuj al-markaz). The commentator argues that this is incorrect and that the correct thickness is double the eccentricity. The commentator first explains that the objection only arises if 'amount' (al-hayz) is interpreted as the actual distance; but if interpreted in the verbal sense as the act of exiting, then the amount obtained by the exit of the center from the center of the world is indeed double the distance between the two centers, so the thickness is double. He then provides a geometric demonstration: supposing points A, B, C on the convexity, the volume of the sphere can be calculated. Finally, he offers an alternative interpretation of the author's phrasing: 'by the amount of eccentricity' means 'according to the eccentricity,' not equality. The thickness is related to the eccentricity such that if the eccentricity is a certain amount, the thickness is twice the earth's diameter. He refers to figures for the eccentric spheres and promises further explanation.

Correction: The correct thickness is double the eccentricity, as proven by demonstration and supported by sound imagination.

Definition of the Epicycle and Its Carrier
The author defines the epicycle and explains its relation to the carrier sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the epicycle in Ptolemaic astronomy, its physical characteristics, and its relationship with the carrier sphere.

Core Idea
The epicycle is a solid sphere embedded in another sphere, tracing a circle as its center moves.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the technical definition of an epicycle, a key component in the Ptolemaic model of planetary motion. The author clarifies that the epicycle is a solid sphere fixed within the thickness of a larger sphere, with its diameter equal to that thickness. He then discusses how its motion relates to the carrier sphere and addresses a potential misunderstanding about the carrier's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the precise definition of the epicycle as a sphere not encompassing Earth.
Notice that the epicycle is distinct from the planet itself.
2
Understand the physical description
Identify that the epicycle is embedded in the thickness of another sphere, with its convexity and concavity touching at points, and its diameter equals that thickness.
The epicycle is assumed solid because a concavity is unnecessary.
3
Grasp the motion of the center
Note that the epicycle's center moves with the carrier sphere, revolving around the world's center, and traces a circle whose center is the carrier's center.
The traced circle is concentric or eccentric depending on the carrier.
4
Consider the carrier's nature
Understand that the carrier sphere can be concentric or eccentric, and that the carrier includes inclined, representative, and eccentric spheres.
This counters the objection that carriers are only eccentric.
5
Reflect on the sun example
Consider the special case where the sun has no eccentric but only an epicycle, making its carrier concentric.
This shows the carrier can be concentric in certain astronomical models.

And the epicycle (al-tadwir) is a sphere other than the planet, not encompassing the earth, but fixed in the thickness of a sphere such that its convexity touches at a point and its concavity at another. Then its diameter equals the thickness of that sphere. And it is not possible for the epicycle to have a concavity, since we have no need for its concavity, so it is assumed to be a solid sphere. Its center moves with the motion of the sphere in whose thickness it is, revolving around the center of the world, and the epicycle traces with its center moving with that motion a circle whose center is the center of the carrier sphere (al-hamil) of the epicycle. Commentary
This passage defines the epicycle (tadwir) and describes its relationship with the carrier sphere. The author states that the epicycle is a sphere distinct from the planet, not surrounding the earth. It is embedded in the thickness of another sphere, with its convexity touching that sphere at one point and its concavity at another, so its diameter equals that sphere's thickness. The epicycle is assumed solid (no concavity) because it is not needed. Its center moves with the motion of the enclosing sphere, revolving around the world's center, and it traces a circle with that moving center. The center of that traced circle is the center of the carrier sphere. If the carrier is concentric, the traced circle is concentric; if eccentric, it is eccentric. The commentator then clarifies that the carrier can be either inclined (mail), representative (mutamthil), or eccentric, so the objection that carriers are only eccentric is unfounded. He also notes that under the view that the sun has no eccentric but only an epicycle, the carrier would be concentric.

Properties of Concentric Motion
Enumeration of properties of concentric sphere motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a concentric sphere moves uniformly, maintaining constant distance from Earth's center and producing equal arcs and angles without any perceived speed variation.

Core Idea
Concentric spheres move uniformly because their distance from Earth's center is constant.

In this passage, the author explains the defining properties of a concentric sphere's motion. Before we study more complex motions, we need to grasp why this simplest case produces perfect uniformity—equal arcs in equal times, equal angles at the center, and no variation in speed or distance. This also sets the stage for understanding why other spheres (like the sun's) behave differently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Note the key claims about arcs, angles, and distance.
The passage is dense; try to visualize the sphere moving around Earth's center.
2
Identify the three main claims
The passage makes three claims about concentric motion: (1) equal arcs in equal times, (2) equal angles at Earth's center, (3) constant distance from Earth. Find the phrases that assert these.
Look for 'قسياً متساوية', 'زوايا متشابهة', and 'متساوي البعد'.
3
Understand why no speed variation occurs
The author argues that because distance is constant, there is no perceived speed or slowness. The commentary adds that Earth's radius is negligible for higher spheres, so no parallax effect. Compare with the sun's sphere where radius matters.
Consider the equinox argument: if there were speed variation, day and night would not be equal.
4
Reflect on the equinox test
The passage ends by pointing to the equality of day and night at equinoxes as proof that the sun's motion (which is not truly concentric but helps illustrate) does not show speed variation from Earth. This reinforces the logic.
The commentator says 'فتأمل' (so reflect) – take a moment to appreciate the reasoning.

حكمه المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثالث : في فلك الثوابت مركزه ( في أزمنة متساوية قسياً متساوية ) من محيط الدائرة التي يتحرك عليها ذلك المتحرك ( ويحدث ) عند مركز الأرض ) زوايا متشابهة ) أي متساوية لأن الحركة البسيطة الواقعة على نهج واحد تقتضي ذلك ) ولا يختلف ) المتحرك على الموافق ( منه ) أي من مركز الأرض (قرباً وبعداً بل يكون دائماً متساوي البعد عنه، لأنه مركز الدائرة التي تتحرك عليها (فلا يحس فيه أي في المتحرك على الموافق بسرعة وبطء لا في مركز الأرض إن فرض هناك إحساس ولا فيما هو ) في كوجه الأرض بالقياس إلى الأفلاك العالية إذ لا قدر لنصف قطر الأرض بالنسبة إليها . قوله : ( إذ لا قدر إلخ بخلاف فلك الشمس وما تحته فإن للأرض بالنسبة إليها قدراً فيتفاوت قرباً وبعداً بقدر نصف قطر الأرض. يكون كوكباً وأن يكون فلك تدوير وأن يكون نقطة من النقط المعتبرة فيما بينهم. وقوله : عند مركز الأرض أي حول مركز الأرض. قوله : ( إن فرض هناك إحساس) إنما قال : إن فرض لأن الإنسان لم يكن ساكناً عند المركز فضلاً عن أن يكون هناك إحساس. وقوله : إذ لا قدر لنصف قطر الأرض بالنسبة إليها يعني أنه لو كان لنصف قطر الأرض بالنسبة إلى الأفلاك العالية قدر لزم أن يحصل لنا الإدراك بالسرعة عند كون ذلك المتحرك فوق الأفق والإدراك بالبطء عند كونه تحت الأفق لأن ذلك المتحرك لا بد أن يكون أقرب بالنسبة إلينا عند كونه فوق الأفق وأن يكون أبعد عند كونه تحت الأفق ويدل على بطلان اللازم كون الليل والنهار متساويين عند حلول الشمس في الاعتدالين فتأمل
Its ruling: The First Observation - Second Chapter - First Section - Third Goal: On the Sphere of the Fixed Stars. Its center traverses equal arcs of the circle on which it moves in equal times, and it generates similar angles at the center of the earth, i.e., equal, because simple motion occurring in one uniform way requires that. And the moving body on the concentric does not vary from it, i.e., from the center of the earth, in nearness and distance, but is always equally distant from it, because it is the center of the circle on which it moves. So no speed or slowness is perceived in the moving body on the concentric, nor at the center of the earth if perception is assumed, nor in what is in the earth's sphere [كوجه?] in relation to the higher spheres, because the radius of the earth has no magnitude relative to them. His saying: '(since no magnitude, etc.)' Unlike the sphere of the sun and what is below it, for the earth has magnitude relative to them, so distance varies by the amount of the earth's radius. It may be a planet, an epicycle, or a point among the considered points. And his saying: 'at the center of the earth' means around the center of the earth. His saying: '(if perception is assumed)' He only said 'if assumed' because man is not stationary at the center, let alone that there is perception. And his saying: 'since the radius of the earth has no magnitude relative to them' means that if the earth's radius had magnitude relative to the higher spheres, then we would perceive speed when the mover is above the horizon and slowness when below the horizon, because the mover would necessarily be nearer to us when above the horizon and farther when below. And the invalidity of the necessary consequence is shown by the equality of night and day when the sun is at the equinoxes, so reflect.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage discusses the properties of a concentric sphere's motion. The author states that the sphere's center moves on a circle around the center of the earth, traversing equal arcs in equal times and generating equal angles at the earth's center. This uniformity follows from the simple nature of the motion. The moving body (the concentric sphere) remains at a constant distance from the earth's center because it moves on a circle centered on that point. Therefore, no variation in speed (faster or slower) is perceptible in such motion, nor is any variation in distance from the earth. The commentator adds an explanation: the earth's radius is negligible compared to the higher spheres, so no parallax effect occurs. He contrasts this with the sphere of the sun and below, where the earth's radius does have magnitude, causing variations in distance. The commentator also clarifies that 'at the center of the earth' means around that center, and the assumption of perception is hypothetical because no one is at the earth's center. The equality of day and night at equinoxes disproves the possibility of such speed variation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think concentric spheres have variable speed or distance from Earth, like planets do.
Correction: The passage shows that because concentric spheres move on circles centered on Earth, their distance remains constant, so no speed variation occurs. This is a fundamental difference from non-concentric spheres.

Key Takeaway
A concentric sphere's motion is perfectly uniform—equal arcs in equal times, equal angles, and constant distance from Earth—because it rotates around Earth's center, and this uniformity is empirically supported by the equality of day and night at equinoxes.

Eccentric Motion and the Points of Perigee and Apogee
Author describes eccentric motion and defines perigee and apogee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eccentric sphere moves uniformly around its own center but appears non-uniform relative to Earth, and you will be able to identify the points of perigee and apogee.

Core Idea
The eccentric sphere is uniform in itself but diverse in relation to the world.

Having established the concentric motion, the commentator now turns to the eccentric sphere. This passage clarifies that while the eccentric moves uniformly around its own centre, its distance from the Earth's centre varies, giving rise to the familiar terms perigee and apogee. Let's read carefully to understand these key concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the content. Note the technical terms introduced.
2
Identify the two halves of the eccentric
The passage describes two halves of the eccentric sphere: one containing the center of the world (nearer to us) and the other farther away. Locate where these halves are described.
Half containing the world's center is nearer; the other is farther.
3
Understand perigee and apogee definitions
Find the definitions of perigee (al-hadid) and apogee (al-awj). Note that perigee is the point of extreme nearness, apogee the point of extreme distance.
These points are where the eccentric touches the concave and convex surfaces of the inclined sphere.
4
Connect to the common misunderstanding
The passage implies that the eccentric's motion is uniform around its own center, but the variation is only relative to the world's center. Reflect on why this might be a common misunderstanding.

قوله: (لما عرفت في الموافق هو قوله : لأن الحركة البسيطة الواقعة على نهج واحد يقتضي ذلك . قوله : أراد به أي أراد بالمائل الفلك الذي يكون الخارج في ثخنه ليندرج فيه الممثلات أيضاً كما مر . وأما الخارج من المركز فإنه لا يختلف منه أي من مركز نفسه ( قرباً وبعداً أو أنه يقطع حول مركز نفسه قسياً وزوايا متشابهة لما عرفت في الموافق (لكنها) أي حركة الخارج ) تختلف بالنسبة إلى مركز العالم لأن أحد نصفيه ) أي نصفي الخارج ( وهو الذي فيه مركز العالم أقرب إلينا وغاية القرب ) منا ( عند نقطة في وسطه ) أي وسط هذا النصف بها ) أي بتلك النقطة ( يماس ) هذا النصف أو الخارج ( مقعر المائل ) أراد به الفلك الذي يكون الخارج في ثخنه كما مر . (وتسمى) هذه النقطة ( الحضيض والنصف الآخر من الخارج ( أبعد منه ) أي من النصف الأول بالقياس إلينا ) وغاية البعد ) بيننا وبينه ( عند نقطة وسطه بها يماس محدب المائل وتسمى )
His saying: '(since you have known in the concentric)' is his saying: 'because simple motion occurring on one course requires that.' His saying: 'He meant by al-mail the sphere in whose thickness the eccentric is located, so that the representatives are also included, as has passed.' And as for the eccentric (al-kharij min al-markaz), it does not vary from it, i.e., from its own center, in nearness and distance, or rather it cuts arcs and angles similar around its own center, as you have known in the concentric. However, its motion, i.e., the motion of the eccentric, differs with respect to the center of the world, because one of its two halves, i.e., the two halves of the eccentric, which contains the center of the world is nearer to us, and the extreme nearness to us is at a point in its middle, i.e., the middle of this half, by which, i.e., by that point, this half or the eccentric touches the concavity of the inclined sphere (al-mail). He meant by al-mail the sphere in whose thickness the eccentric is located, as has passed. And this point is called perigee (al-hadid). And the other half of the eccentric is farther than it, i.e., than the first half in relation to us, and the extreme distance between us and it is at a point in its middle by which it touches the convexity of the inclined sphere, and it is called apogee (al-awj).
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of celestial motions, focusing on the eccentric sphere. The commentator first connects back to the earlier explanation of the concentric: simple motion on a single course generates uniform arcs and angles. He clarifies that 'al-mail' (the inclined sphere) is the sphere in whose thickness the eccentric lies, and this includes the 'representatives' (al-mutamaththilat). The author then explains the eccentric itself: it does not vary in distance from its own center—it is uniform relative to that center—and it cuts similar arcs and angles around its own center, just like the concentric. However, relative to the center of the world (i.e., the earth), its motion varies because the eccentric is not centered on the earth. One half of the eccentric contains the earth's center, making that half nearer to us. The point of extreme nearness is where the eccentric touches the concavity of the inclined sphere; this point is called perigee (al-hadid). The other half is farther, and its point of extreme distance touches the convexity of the inclined sphere; that point is called apogee (al-awj).

Concentric Motion of the Fixed Stars
Description of uniform concentric celestial motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a celestial body in concentric motion appears uniform and exhibits no speed variation from Earth's perspective.

Core Idea
Concentric motion is uniform and equidistant from Earth, so no speed change is perceived.

The celestial bodies move in complex ways, but some follow simple concentric circles around the Earth. In this lesson, we examine how a body moving on a concentric circle behaves uniformly and why, from our vantage point, it shows no variation in speed or distance. This foundational concept sets the stage for understanding more intricate motions like eccentric and epicyclic paths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on concentric motion
Read the Arabic passage describing concentric motion and its translation. Notice that the body moves on a circle centered at Earth, cutting equal arcs in equal times.
The passage emphasizes uniformity and equality of arcs and angles.
2
Identify why no speed variation occurs
The text explains that because the distance from Earth is constant, there is no perceived speed difference. Reflect on why this is so.
Think about the relationship between distance and apparent speed.
3
Consider the relative insignificance of Earth's radius
The passage mentions that Earth's radius is negligible compared to the celestial spheres. How does this affect perception?
This reinforces that concentric motion appears perfectly uniform.

Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory section from al-Jurjani's commentary on al-Mawaqif, discussing the motions of celestial bodies in the sphere of fixed stars. The author first describes the motion of a body on a concentric circle (al-muwāfiq): it moves uniformly, at constant distance from the center, so no change in speed is perceived relative to the earth. Then he turns to the eccentric (al-khārij), which moves uniformly around its own center but appears to vary in speed from the earth's perspective because one half (containing the earth's center) is nearer, causing larger apparent arcs, and the other half is farther, causing smaller apparent arcs. The points of extreme nearness and distance are called perigee (al- ḥaḍīḍ) and apogee (al-awj). In the apogee half, the body traces a smaller arc and angle (arc by vision, angle actually), appearing slower; in the perigee half, a larger arc and angle, appearing faster. The commentary clarifies that arcs are perceived according to vision (size with distance) while angles are physically smaller or larger. Next, the author explains epicyclic motion (al-tadwīr). An epicycle does not encompass the earth; its motion in one half is forward (parallel to the carrier), so the two motions add and the body appears faster. In the other half, the epicyclic motion is opposite, so the net motion is the excess of the carrier's motion, appearing slower. If the epicyclic motion equals the carrier's, the body appears stationary; if it exceeds, the body appears retrograde. The commentary elaborates that these phenomena occur in real eccentrepicycles (al-muḥayyaza), citing the carrier's forward pull and the epicycle's opposite pull causing hesitation, and clarifies that arcs and angles are understood as in the previous discussion.

Concentric motion is uniform and equidistant, so no speed variation is perceived from Earth.

Eccentric Motion: Perigee and Apogee
Explanation of eccentric motion with perigee and apogee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how eccentric motion creates an apparent variation in speed and what is meant by perigee and apogee.

Core Idea
Eccentric motion causes apparent speed changes because one half is nearer to Earth than the other.

Unlike concentric motion, an eccentric circle is not centered on Earth. This leads to a fascinating effect: the same uniform motion around the eccentric's own center appears faster when the body is nearer to Earth and slower when farther. The points of nearest and farthest approach are called perigee and apogee. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning behind this apparent speed variation and the role of vision and actual angles.

Guided Path
1
Read the eccentric motion passage
Read the Arabic passage describing eccentric motion and its translation. Notice that the body moves uniformly around its own center.
The key is that the Earth is not at the center of the eccentric circle.
2
Identify perigee and apogee
Find where the passage introduces the points of perigee (nearest to Earth) and apogee (farthest). Understand why these points are defined.
These points are at the middle of the nearer and farther halves of the eccentric.
3
Understand why speed appears to vary
The passage explains that in the apogee half, the body traces a smaller arc (as seen from Earth) and a smaller actual angle, making it appear slower. In the perigee half, the opposite occurs. Reflect on the distinction between vision and reality.
The commentary clarifies that arcs are seen according to distance, while angles are actual.

Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory section from al-Jurjani's commentary on al-Mawaqif, discussing the motions of celestial bodies in the sphere of fixed stars. The author first describes the motion of a body on a concentric circle (al-muwāfiq): it moves uniformly, at constant distance from the center, so no change in speed is perceived relative to the earth. Then he turns to the eccentric (al-khārij), which moves uniformly around its own center but appears to vary in speed from the earth's perspective because one half (containing the earth's center) is nearer, causing larger apparent arcs, and the other half is farther, causing smaller apparent arcs. The points of extreme nearness and distance are called perigee (al- ḥaḍīḍ) and apogee (al-awj). In the apogee half, the body traces a smaller arc and angle (arc by vision, angle actually), appearing slower; in the perigee half, a larger arc and angle, appearing faster. The commentary clarifies that arcs are perceived according to vision (size with distance) while angles are physically smaller or larger. Next, the author explains epicyclic motion (al-tadwīr). An epicycle does not encompass the earth; its motion in one half is forward (parallel to the carrier), so the two motions add and the body appears faster. In the other half, the epicyclic motion is opposite, so the net motion is the excess of the carrier's motion, appearing slower. If the epicyclic motion equals the carrier's, the body appears stationary; if it exceeds, the body appears retrograde. The commentary elaborates that these phenomena occur in real eccentrepicycles (al-muḥayyaza), citing the carrier's forward pull and the epicycle's opposite pull causing hesitation, and clarifies that arcs and angles are understood as in the previous discussion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The celestial body actually changes speed in eccentric motion.
Correction: The body moves uniformly around its own center; only the apparent speed from Earth varies due to distance.

Key Takeaway

Epicyclic Motion: Forward, Stationary, and Retrograde
Explanation of epicyclic motion and its effects on apparent motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how epicyclic motion explains the apparent forward, stationary, and retrograde motions of planets.

Core Idea
Epicyclic motion combines with carrier motion to produce forward, stationary, or retrograde apparent motion.

Beyond eccentric circles, ancient astronomers used epicycles—small circles whose centers move along a larger orbit—to account for the complex motions of planets. In this lesson, we examine how the direction of epicyclic motion relative to its carrier leads to three distinct observations: faster forward motion, slower forward motion, apparent stationarity, and even retrograde (backward) motion. This elegant model explains phenomena that simple concentric or eccentric motion cannot.

Guided Path
1
Read the epicycle passage
Read the Arabic passage on epicyclic motion and its translation. Focus on the two halves of the epicycle and their relationship to the carrier.
The epicycle's center moves on the carrier (eccentric or concentric).
2
Identify the three cases
The passage describes three outcomes: faster (sum of motions), slower (difference), stationary (equal), and retrograde (epicycle faster). Identify each and understand the conditions.
These correspond to forward, stationary, and backward motion in the sky.
3
Understand the role of the eccentrepicycle
The commentary mentions 'eccentrepicycle' (al-muḥayyaza). Note that these phenomena actually occur in the eccentrepicycle model, not just epicycle alone.
This is a combination of eccentric and epicycle.

Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory section from al-Jurjani's commentary on al-Mawaqif, discussing the motions of celestial bodies in the sphere of fixed stars. The author first describes the motion of a body on a concentric circle (al-muwāfiq): it moves uniformly, at constant distance from the center, so no change in speed is perceived relative to the earth. Then he turns to the eccentric (al-khārij), which moves uniformly around its own center but appears to vary in speed from the earth's perspective because one half (containing the earth's center) is nearer, causing larger apparent arcs, and the other half is farther, causing smaller apparent arcs. The points of extreme nearness and distance are called perigee (al- ḥaḍīḍ) and apogee (al-awj). In the apogee half, the body traces a smaller arc and angle (arc by vision, angle actually), appearing slower; in the perigee half, a larger arc and angle, appearing faster. The commentary clarifies that arcs are perceived according to vision (size with distance) while angles are physically smaller or larger. Next, the author explains epicyclic motion (al-tadwīr). An epicycle does not encompass the earth; its motion in one half is forward (parallel to the carrier), so the two motions add and the body appears faster. In the other half, the epicyclic motion is opposite, so the net motion is the excess of the carrier's motion, appearing slower. If the epicyclic motion equals the carrier's, the body appears stationary; if it exceeds, the body appears retrograde. The commentary elaborates that these phenomena occur in real eccentrepicycles (al-muḥayyaza), citing the carrier's forward pull and the epicycle's opposite pull causing hesitation, and clarifies that arcs and angles are understood as in the previous discussion.

Key Terms
التدوير
الحامل
المتحيزة
رجوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Retrograde motion is caused by planets actually slowing down and reversing direction.
Correction: Retrograde motion is an apparent effect due to the epicycle's motion exceeding the carrier's, causing the planet to appear to move backward from Earth.

Checkpoint
What does it mean when the epicycle's motion equals the carrier's motion?
A) The planet appears stationary.
B) The planet appears faster.
C) The planet appears retrograde.
D) The planet appears slower.

Key Takeaway
Epicyclic motion explains forward, slower, stationary, and retrograde apparent motions by combining with the carrier's motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How might this lesson on epicyclic motion help you reinterpret those periods as part of a greater, hidden forward motion? What would it take for you to trust that process today?
?

How can you remind yourself of the underlying forward motion and avoid discouragement? What concrete step will you take to focus on the carrier motion rather than the apparent backwardness?

Motion in Apogee and Perigee: Arc and Angle
Explains why planets appear slower at apogee and faster at perigee using arc/angle distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why planets appear slower at apogee and faster at perigee, based on the distinction between the arc as seen by sight and the angle as it actually is.

Core Idea
Apparent speed is a geometric illusion, not a change in actual motion.

This passage from a classical astronomical text addresses a key observation: planets seem to slow down and speed up in their orbits. The author explains that this is due to the geometry of viewing angle and distance, not a change in the planet's true motion. By distinguishing between the arc (what we see) and the angle (what is real), the mystery is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the gist.
Notice the technical terms: الأوج (apogee), الحضيض (perigee), القوس (arc), الزاوية (angle).
2
Identify the main claim
Look for the claim about apogee and perigee: what happens to the arc and angle, and how does that affect apparent speed?
The passage says at apogee: smaller arc and angle; at perigee: larger arc and angle.
3
Understand the arc/angle distinction
The annotation explains that the arc is related to sight (distance makes things appear small), while the angle is related to actual geometry. Understand why both are smaller at apogee but for different reasons.
Arc apparent due to distance; angle actual due to side lengths.
4
Connect to the overall explanation
Combine the two halves: the geometric effect causes the planet to appear slower when farther and faster when nearer, even though its true motion may be constant.
This resolves the apparent anomaly.

First Observatory – Chapter Two: First Section: Third Purpose: Concerning the Sphere of the Fixed Stars. This point (the apogee draws) the outer [body] moving with its motion in an amount of time, and it is in the apogee half a smaller arc and angle: as for the arc, it is according to sight, and as for the angle, it is according to the actual state. Thus that moving body is seen slower. And it draws in that amount of time in the perigee half a larger arc and angle analogously to what preceded, so the moving body is seen faster, because if the times of two motions are united and their distances differ, the motion whose distance is longer is necessarily faster. His saying: 'As for the arc, it is according to sight' because one thing, when it is near, appears large, and when it is distant, appears small. His saying: 'And as for the angle, it is according to the actual state' because the angle whose two sides are longer is actually smaller than the angle whose two sides are shorter, even if their chords are equal.

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the effect of apogee and perigee on apparent speed. The author states that the moving body at apogee traces a smaller arc and angle, but then clarifies the distinction: the arc is perceived by sight (so it appears smaller due to distance), while the angle is truly smaller in reality. This explains why the planet appears slower at apogee. Similarly, at perigee, the arc and angle are larger, so the planet appears faster. The proof is that with equal time, longer distance implies faster motion. The annotation (قوله) further explains the arc and angle: the arc is a matter of sight because distance affects apparent size, while the angle is actual because the length of sides affects the angle magnitude even if the chord is the same.

Epicycle Motion and Apparent Speed Variations
Describes how epicycle motion causes faster, slower, stationary, and retrograde appearances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how epicycle motion combined with carrier motion produces apparent variations in planetary speed, including stationary and retrograde appearances.

Core Idea
Epicycle motion explains apparent speed changes, stationarity, and retrogradation.

This passage explains the Ptolemaic model's epicycle mechanism for planetary motion. It shows how the combination of epicycle and carrier motions yields observed phenomena: faster, slower, stationary, and retrograde appearances. Understanding this is essential for grasping medieval astronomy's explanation of planetary behavior.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the technical terms like 'التدوير', 'الحامل', 'التوالي', and 'خلاف التوالي'.
2
Identify the two halves of the epicycle
The passage describes motion in one half agreeing with the carrier and the other half opposing. Find where these are mentioned and note which half corresponds to faster vs. slower apparent motion.
The annotation specifies the upper half for planets and lower half for the Moon/Sun.
3
Examine the three special cases
The passage lists three outcomes: faster, slower, and then equality leading to stationary, and excess leading to retrograde. Understand the condition for each.
Equality means epicycle motion cancels carrier's motion, causing stationarity.
4
Reflect on apparent vs. actual motion
Consider that stationary and retrograde are apparent, not actual reversals. The carrier's motion still continues, but the combined effect creates the illusion.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : ( بل ربما إلخ) وذلك إنما يكون في المتحيزة واقعاً لأن الحامل بحركته جر إلى التوالي والتدوير جر إلى خلاف التوالي فتحيز في موضع واحد من الفلك البروج كأنه لا يتحرك .

As for the epicycle, since it does not encompass the Earth, its motion in one of its halves is in the same direction as its carrier, i.e., agreeing with its motion in direction. So if a moving body moves by epicycle motion in that half and the center of the epicycle also moves by the carrier's motion, the two motions are in one direction. Thus the sensed [motion] in that moving body is the sum of its motion (i.e., epicycle motion) and its carrier's motion, so it is seen faster. And its motion in the other half is opposite to the carrier's direction, so the sensed [motion] in that moving body is the excess of its carrier's motion over its motion, so it is seen slower. And perhaps it equals it, i.e., the epicycle equals its carrier in motion sensibly, so the carrier's motion has no excess, so that moving body is seen stationary in a part of the zodiacal belt, not leaving its alignment for a period. And perhaps the epicycle exceeds its carrier in motion, so that moving body is seen retrograde from the direction towards which it was moving. His saying: 'in one of its halves': it is the upper half of the planet and the lower half for the Moon and Sun at the top of the epicycle. His saying: 'perhaps etc.': that only actually occurs in planets, because the carrier by its motion pulls in the same direction and the epicycle pulls in the opposite direction, so it remains in one place of the celestial sphere as if it does not move. His saying: 'and perhaps the epicycle exceeds it': that also occurs in planets.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage explains the effects of epicycle motion on apparent speed. The author first states the rule for the half where epicycle motion agrees with the carrier: the motions add, so the planet appears faster. For the opposite half, the epicycle motion opposes the carrier, so only the excess of the carrier's motion is observed, resulting in slower motion. Then three special cases: if the epicycle motion exactly equals the carrier's motion, the planet appears stationary; if the epicycle exceeds, it appears retrograde. The annotations clarify which half is which and explain the stationary case as resulting from two opposing pulls.

Detailed Clarification of Arc and Angle Distinction
Provides deeper explanation of arc by sight versus angle by reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the arc is considered according to sight while the angle is according to actual state, and how a single arc can correspond to two different angles.

Core Idea
The actual arc is constant, but the angle varies with side lengths.

This lesson deepens the earlier distinction between arc and angle by explaining their different bases: arc is judged by appearance, while angle is judged by reality. The passage shows how the same arc can serve as chord for two angles of different sizes, proving that the angle is a truer measure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the contrast between the treatment of arc (by sight) and angle (by actual state).
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms القوس (arc), الزاوية (angle), الوتر (chord), and نفس الأمر (actual state). Understand their roles.
The term 'chord' connects the arc and angle.
3
Follow the reasoning
Trace the argument: arcs are equal in actual state, but the same arc can be a chord for two angles with different side lengths, making one angle actually smaller.
This shows why angle is a better measure of actual state.
4
Connect to broader context
Relate this to the earlier discussion about epicycle halves and the difference in angle at apogee and perigee.
The angle at apogee is smaller despite the arc appearing similar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'As for the arc, it is according to sight' i.e., not according to actual state, for the arcs of the sphere do not differ in actual state as previously mentioned. Middle Motion, Stationary Points, and Apogee/Perigee in Epicycle Theory
Explains middle motion and stationary points bounding direct and retrograde motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how middle motion, stationary points, and the apogee and perigee are defined in epicycle theory, and why the farther half is seen slower.

Core Idea
Stationary points flank both direct and retrograde motion.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from a classical astronomical text that explains the relationship between speed, slowness, and stationary points in epicycle motion. The author clarifies that when the epicycle moves with neither equality nor excess, a middle motion arises; when excess is present, direct and retrograde motions are each bounded by two stationary points. The passage also identifies the apogee and perigee of the epicycle and corrects a common misperception about the speed of the farther half.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Pay attention to the technical terms: middle motion, stationary points, apogee, perigee.
The passage is dense; it may help to read the commentary first.
2
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage discusses two cases: one with no equality or excess (leading to a middle motion) and one with excess (leading to direct and retrograde motions flanked by stationary points). Locate these in the text.
3
Understand the apogee and perigee
The passage explains that one half of the epicycle is farther from us, and its midpoint is the apogee (the farthest distance). The other half is nearer, with its midpoint as the perigee. The farther half is seen slower, the nearer half faster.
This corrects a claim that the farther half should be seen faster.
4
Review the annotation for details
The annotation provides additional conditions: when the planet is at the top of the epicycle it is direct and fast; as it descends it becomes slow; when motions equal, it is stationary; when excess, retrograde. This helps solidify the main ideas.

قوله : ( وهو أن لا يكون هناك مساواة بل نقصان وذلك في القمر والشمس على أصل التدوير . قوله: (ويستقيم إلخ) بيان ذلك أنه إذا كانت أحد الكواكب المتحيزة في أعلى تدويره كانت حركة مركزه موافقة لحركة مركز تدويره على توالي البروج فيرى مستقيما سريع الحركة، وإذا قرب الكوكب إلى أسفل التدوير جعل ميلاً إلى خلاف التوالي لكنه ما دام حركة مركز الكوكب إلى الخلاف أقل في الرؤية من حركة مركز التدوير مستقيماً لكنه بطيء السير. فإذا تساويا يرى مستقيماً لتعارض الحركتين وإذا زادت حركة مركز الكواكب إلى الخلاف على حركة التدوير إلى التوالي يرى راجعاً بتدرج من البطء إلى السرعة في الرجوع ثم من السرعة إلى البطء وأيضاً ثم يقيم بعد تمام الرجعة . ثانياً : إذا تساوت الحركتان ويستقيم بعد الإقامة لأمور معينة.
•
قوله : ( ولأنه يتدرج) متعلق في المعنى بما بعده من قوله فيكون بينهما إلخ . وكذا قوله : ولأنه يرجع متعلق بحسب المعنى بما بعده من قوله : فيكون كل منهما إلخ . ولو قال : يكون بترك الفاء لكان أهون والذي يقال في أمثاله هو أنه يقدر يكون عاملاً في الظرف. ويجعل قوله : فيكون منيراً لذلك المقدر. وقوله : وذلك إلخ، أي التدرج من سرعة إلى بطء إنما يكون على تقدير أن لا تكون هناك مساواة ولا زيادة أصلا فإنه على تقدير المساواة يلزم الوقوف وعلى تقدير الزيادة يلزم الرجوع وأما على تقدير أن لا يكون هناك زيادة ولا مساواة فيتصور أن يكون هناك سرعة عند كون الكوكب في النصف الموافق وبطء عند كونه في النصف المخالف بأن يكون المحسوس هناك فضل حركة حاملة على حركته كما عرفت . قوله : ( أسرع كما زعمه هلا يمكن أن يريد المصنف بالأسرعية هاهنا ) مجازاً إذ المعقول أن يرى القوس أقصر لا أن يرى أبطأ فإن القوس من قبيل المقادير لا من قبيل الحركات حتى يرى أسرع أو أبطأ.
معنی
الأقصرية
[Epicycle motion is] in a direction opposite to it. And because it (i.e., the epicycle) gradually moves the moving body on it from speed in the half agreeing with the carrier to slowness in the other half, and that according to the first estimation, which is that there is no equality nor excess of epicycle motion. Then there is between them a middle motion, because it returns to opposition after direct motion, and it becomes direct again after retrograde, and that on the estimation of excess of epicycle motion. Then each of them (direct and retrograde) is flanked by two stationary points: one at the end of direct and beginning of retrograde, and the other the opposite. Also, one of the two halves of the epicycle is farther from us, so the arc cut from it (the farther slower half) is seen not faster as he claimed, because the implication of distance in itself is without faster. And its midpoint (i.e., the midpoint of the mentioned half) is the farthest distance relative to the center of the world, and that midpoint is called the apogee. And the other half is nearer to us, so the arc cut from it is seen faster, not slower, and its midpoint (i.e., the midpoint of the other half) is the perigee. His saying: 'that there is no equality but deficiency', that is for the Moon and Sun according to the original epicycle. His saying: 'and it becomes direct etc.': explanation of that: when one of the planets is at the top of its epicycle, its center's motion agrees with its epicycle center's motion on the order of the zodiac, so it is seen direct with fast motion. When the planet approaches the bottom of the epicycle, it inclines opposite direction, but as long as the planet's center's motion towards opposition is less in sight than the epicycle center's motion, it is seen direct but slow. If they equal, it is seen stationary due to the two motions opposing each other. If the planet's center's motion towards opposition exceeds the epicycle's motion towards the order, it is seen retrograde with gradual increase from slow to fast during retrograde, then from fast to slow, and then it stations after completion of the retrograde arc. Secondly: if the two motions equal, it becomes direct after station for certain reasons. His saying: 'and because it gradually' is related in meaning to what follows from his saying 'then there is between them' etc. Similarly his saying: 'and because it returns' is related in meaning to what follows from his saying 'then each of them' etc. And if he had said 'then there is' without the 'fa', it would have been easier, and what is said in such cases is that he estimates 'to be' as an operator in the adverbial. And he makes his saying 'then there is' as a clarifier of that estimated. And his saying: 'that is' etc., i.e., the gradation from speed to slowness only occurs on the estimation that there is no equality nor excess at all. For on the estimation of equality, station follows, and on the estimation of excess, retrograde follows. But on the estimation that there is no excess nor equality, it is conceivable that there is speed when the planet is in the agreeing half and slowness when it is in the opposing half, such that the sensible there is the excess of its carrier's motion over its motion, as you have known. His saying: 'faster as he claimed' — it may be that the author intended by 'faster' here figuratively, for what is understood is that the arc is seen shorter, not that it is seen slower, for the arc is of the category of magnitudes, not of motions, such that it would be seen faster or slower. Meaning: the shorter.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage describes the gradual transition in epicycle motion. When there is no equality or excess, there is a middle motion between speed and slowness. When there is excess, direct and retrograde motions are each flanked by two stationary points. The passage also corrects a claim: the farther half should be seen slower, not faster. The midpoint of the farther half is the apogee, and of the nearer half is the perigee. The annotation provides detailed conditions: when the planet is at the epicycle's top, it is direct and fast; as it descends, it becomes slow; if the motion of the planet's center equals that of the epicycle center, it is stationary; if the planet's motion exceeds, it becomes retrograde with gradual speed changes. The annotation also clarifies that the term 'faster' may be figurative, as arcs are magnitudes, not motions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That retrograde motion is continuous and not bounded by stationary points.
Correction: The passage states that both direct and retrograde motions are flanked by two stationary points, so retrograde motion is bounded by stationarity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many stationary points flank each direct and retrograde motion?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
In epicycle theory, stationary points serve as boundaries between direct and retrograde motion, and the farther half is seen slower due to distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, we learn that distance alone implies slowness, not speed. When you feel distant from Allah, do you assume that your spiritual journey is still fast? What will you do today to measure your speed by honest proximity to Him, not by deceptive distance?
?
Stationary points mark the boundaries between direct and retrograde motion. Have you ever experienced a period of spiritual stillness that seemed pointless? Could it have been a stationary point where Allah was preparing you to change your direction? Commit to re-examine a past period of stillness as a possible boundary.
?
The author corrects a misconception that the farther half appears faster. What misconceptions about your own spiritual motion do you hold? Are you willing to let go of the illusion of speed and accept that you may need to move back toward the Source?

Defining the Moon's Nodes
Defines the nodes and names them head and tail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the moon's nodes are, how they are defined, and why they are called 'head' and 'tail'.

Core Idea
The moon's orbit intersects the ecliptic at two points called nodes.

In this passage, the author explains the concept of the moon's nodes, which are the two intersection points of the moon's orbital plane with the ecliptic. He also gives a vivid analogy comparing the shape to a dragon, naming the ascending node 'head' and the descending node 'tail'. This foundational definition sets the stage for understanding lunar motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of nodes
Read the part where the author explains that the moon's orbit cuts the ecliptic into two halves at two points called nodes (العقدتان).
2
Identify the head and tail
Notice which node is called the head (الرأس) and which is the tail (الذنب), based on the moon's direction after passing.
The head is where the moon goes north, the tail where it goes south.
3
Understand the dragon analogy
Reflect on why the author likens the intersecting half-circles to a dragon and its ends to head and tail.
This visual metaphor helps remember the shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الخامس : أفلاك القمر مقابلة لتوسط الأرض على هذا التقدير بينه وبين الشمس في المقابلات كلها فيقع القمر في ظل الأرض في كل منها. واللازم منتف بل تقاطعه أي تقاطع منطقة المائل فلك البروج ونقطعه بنصفين على نقطتين يسميان العقدتين والجوزهرين إحداهما هي التي إذا جاوزها القمر حصل في الشمال من منطقة البروج. وتسمى هذه النقطة الرأس والنقطة الأخرى منهما هي مقابلتها التي إذا جاوزها القمر حصل في الجنوب من فلك البروج وتسمى الذنب بناء على تشبيه الشكل الحادث من نصفي الدائرتين المتقاطعتين بالتنين وتشبيه طرفيه برأسه وذنبه ثم إذا رصدنا كسوفاً في إحدى العقدتين كالرأس مثلاً ثم كسوفاً آخر فيها بعد زمان طويل رأينا الثاني من الكسوفين متأخراً عن الأول إلى جهة المغرب من أجزاء فلك البروج. فعلمنا بذلك أن للعقدتين حركة إلى خلاف التوالي فله أي للقمر فلك آخر سوى الثلاثة المذكورة يحركهما أي يحرك ذلك الفلك الآخر العقدتين إلى خلاف التوالي ولظهور حركته في الجوزهرين سميناه فلك الجوزهر
The first observatory - Chapter Two: First Section: Fifth Objective: The spheres of the moon. (The moon's) opposite (positions) are due to the earth being between it and the sun in all oppositions, so the moon falls in the earth's shadow in each of them. But the necessary consequence is negated; rather, it (the moon's orbit) intersects the inclined sphere's region (the ecliptic) and cuts it into two halves at two points called the two nodes and the two jawzahars. One of them is the point which when the moon passes it, it becomes in the north of the ecliptic. This point is called the head, and the other point is its opposite, which when the moon passes it, it becomes in the south of the ecliptic, and it is called the tail, based on likening the shape resulting from the two intersecting half-circles to a dragon (tinnin) and likening its two ends to its head and tail. Then if we observe an eclipse at one of the two nodes, such as the head, then another eclipse at it after a long time, we see the second of the two eclipses delayed from the first towards the west (maghrib) with respect to the parts of the ecliptic. So we know by that that the two nodes have a motion opposite to the sequence of signs (khilāf al-tawālī). So the moon has another sphere besides the three mentioned, which moves them (i.e., that other sphere moves the two nodes opposite to the sequence of signs). And due to the appearance of its motion in the two jawzahars, we called it the sphere of jawzahar.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the two nodes (العقدتان) of the moon and provides proof for their westward motion. It begins by explaining that lunar eclipses occur when the earth is between the moon and the sun. The moon's orbital plane intersects the ecliptic at two points called the nodes. The ascending node (الرأس) is where the moon crosses into the north, and the descending node (الذنب) into the south. These are named after the head and tail of a dragon (التنين). The proof of westward motion comes from observing two eclipses at the same node over a long interval: the second eclipse occurs westward of the first, indicating the node has moved westward (خلاف التوالي). This motion requires a separate sphere (فلك الجوزهر) that moves the nodes westward. The passage systematically presents: the definition of nodes, the naming, the observational proof, and the inference of a dedicated sphere.

Key Terms
العقدتان
الرأس
الذنب
التنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the nodes are fixed points in the sky.
Correction: The nodes are defined by the intersection of orbits and can move, as the next lesson will show.

Checkpoint
Which node is called the 'head'?
A) The node where the moon goes north.
B) The node where the moon goes south.
C) The node where eclipses occur.
D) The node opposite to the dragon's tail.

Key Takeaway
The moon's orbital intersections with the ecliptic are called nodes; the ascending node is the head, the descending node is the tail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that the moon's path is simple and unchanging? How does knowing about the nodes shift your awareness of the precision behind every lunar eclipse? Will you pause next time you see a partial eclipse to remember the hidden geometry?
?
The early astronomers used a dragon to visualize the nodes. What metaphors do you use to understand complex realities? Are you willing to admit when a mental picture is just an approximation, not the full truth?
?
This lesson shows that careful observation (eclipses not monthly) corrected a mistaken assumption. Is there an area in your life where you cling to a comfortable assumption despite evidence? What would it cost you to reexamine it?

Proof of Westward Motion of the Nodes
Proves westward motion of nodes via eclipse observation and infers a sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the observational evidence for the westward motion of the moon's nodes and the inference of a dedicated sphere.

Core Idea
Eclipse observations show the nodes drift westward over time.

After defining the nodes, the author now provides concrete observational proof that the nodes move. By comparing two eclipses at the same node separated by a long interval, we see the second eclipse occurs westward relative to the first. This demonstrates westward motion (khilāf al-tawālī) and leads to positing a separate sphere that moves the nodes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the observation
Read the description of observing two eclipses at the same node (e.g., head) after a long time.
The second eclipse appears west of the first.
2
Interpret the result
Realize that this westward shift indicates the node itself has moved westward relative to the fixed stars.
This motion is called 'opposite to the sequence' (خلاف التوالي).
3
Infer the sphere
The author concludes there must be a separate sphere (the sphere of jawzahar) that moves the nodes.
This sphere is independent of the moon's other spheres.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Moon's Latitude: Nodes, Maxima, and Symmetry
Describes the moon's latitude variation relative to the ecliptic, with maximum 5 degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moon's latitude relative to the ecliptic changes from zero at the nodes to a fixed maximum of 5 degrees, and how the pattern is symmetrical between north and south.

Core Idea
The moon's latitude oscillates between zero and a constant 5 degrees in a symmetrical cycle.

This passage explains the precise motion of the moon's latitude as it travels through its orbit. You will see how the moon starts with no latitude at the ascending node, gradually increases to a northern maximum, then decreases back to zero at the descending node, and repeats the pattern in the south. The passage also emphasizes that the maximum latitude is fixed at 5 degrees by observation, and the increase and decrease are proportional and symmetrical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the sequence of locations: head, midpoint, tail, other midpoint.
The passage uses technical terms like الرأس (head) and الذنب (tail) for the nodes.
2
Trace the latitude cycle
Follow the moon's path: from head (zero latitude) to northern increase, to maximum at the northern midpoint, then decrease to tail (zero), then southern increase to maximum, then decrease back to head.
Notice that the maximum at the northern and southern midpoints are equal and fixed.
3
Understand the symmetry
The passage states that the increasing and decreasing latitudes are equal in corresponding parts on opposite sides. Verify this relationship with the example given.
This symmetry implies a uniform rate of change.
4
Confirm the constant maximum
The maximum latitude on both sides is five degrees, as determined by observation. This is a key empirical fact in Islamic astronomy.

فالقمر إذا وصل إلى الرأس كان على منطقة البروج فلم يكن له حينئذ عرض ثم إذا جاوزه كان له عرض عن المنطقة في الشمال يتزايد قليلاً قليلاً إلى أن يصل إلى منتصف ما بين العقدتين، وعنده يكون غاية العرض الشمالي ثم يتناقص قليلا قليلا إلى أن يحصل في الذنب فيكون حينئذ عديم العرض أيضا ثم يصير ذا عرض في الجنوب كما وصفناه فيتزايد أولا إلى أن يصل إلى المنتصف الآخر فيكون هناك غاية العرض الجنوبي ويتناقص وغاية العرض في الجانبين أي الشمال والجنوب سواء ثابت لا يزيد ولا ينقص ومقدارها كما علم بالرصد خمسة أجزاء والتزايد في العرض بعد مجاوزة العقدتين والتناقص فيه بعد مجاوزة المنتصفين بنسبة واحدة فهي أي العروض المتزايدة والمتناقصة متساوية في الأجزاء المتقابلة فالعرض المتزايد الشمالي للجزء العاشر من الرأس مثلاً يساوي العرض المتزايد الجنوبي للعاشر من الذنب وكذا العرض المتناقص الشمالي للجزء الخامس من منتصف النصف الشمالي يساوي العرض المتناقص الجنوبي للجزء الخامس من المنتصف الآخر.
So when the moon reaches the head, it is on the ecliptic and thus has no latitude; then when it passes it, it has latitude from the ecliptic in the north, increasing little by little until it reaches the midpoint between the two nodes, and at that point it has the maximum northern latitude. Then it decreases little by little until it arrives at the tail, so then it has no latitude again; then it becomes having latitude in the south as we described, so it increases first until it reaches the other midpoint, where it has the maximum southern latitude, and then decreases. And the maximum latitude on both sides, i.e., north and south, is equal and constant, neither increasing nor decreasing, and its amount, as known by observation, is five degrees. And the increase in latitude after passing the nodes and the decrease after passing the midpoints are in the same proportion, so the increasing and decreasing latitudes are equal in corresponding parts: the northern increasing latitude for the tenth part from the head, for example, equals the southern increasing latitude for the tenth part from the tail. Likewise, the northern decreasing latitude for the fifth part from the midpoint of the northern half equals the southern decreasing latitude for the fifth part from the other midpoint.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage describes how the moon's latitude (العرض) relative to the ecliptic changes as it moves through its orbit. Starting at the ascending node (الرأس), the moon has zero latitude. As it moves northward, its latitude increases gradually until it reaches a maximum at the midpoint between the nodes (the quadrature point). Then it decreases back to zero at the descending node (الذنب). The pattern repeats in the southern hemisphere, with maximum southern latitude at the opposite midpoint. The maximum latitude in both directions is constant at 5 degrees, as determined by observation. The increase and decrease are proportional, and there is symmetry: the latitude at a given fraction of the arc from a node on one side equals the corresponding latitude on the other side. The passage emphasizes precision: the maximum is constant, and the rates are uniform.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the maximum latitude on both sides is equal and constant, and its amount is five degrees, as determined by observation.

The Moon's Four Spheres and Four Motions
Summarizes the four spheres of the moon and their respective motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four spheres of the moon (epicycle, deferent, inclined, and jawzahar) and their four distinct motions according to the Ptolemaic model.

Core Idea
The moon's motion is governed by four nested spheres, each with its own motion.

This passage from al-Mawaqif summarizes the complex Ptolemaic lunar model in a compact form. It lists the four spheres that carry the moon and describes how each moves. Understanding this summary requires careful attention to the hierarchy of spheres and their motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style: each sphere is described briefly.
2
Identify the four spheres
The passage lists four spheres. Find each one: epicycle (تدوير), deferent (خارج), inclined (مائل), and jawzahar (جوزهر). Note their arrangement: the epicycle is embedded in the deferent, which lies in the inclined sphere, which is surrounded by the jawzahar sphere.
The inclined sphere is actually a concentric sphere, but called inclined due to its tilted plane.
3
Distinguish the motions
The passage assigns a motion to each sphere. The epicycle has two half-motions (eastward in lower half, westward in upper). The deferent moves eastward entirely. The inclined and jawzahar both move westward.
Remember: 'to the sequence' means eastward; 'opposite' means westward.
4
Visualize the nested model
Imagine concentric spheres: the epicycle is a small sphere inside the deferent, which is an eccentric sphere inside the inclined sphere, which is inside the jawzahar sphere. The moon sits on the epicycle.
This model accounts for the moon's irregular apparent motion.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Ptolemaic astronomers needed four spheres. The answer lies in explaining the moon's complex motion, including its varying speed and latitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد تلخص مما ذكرناه أن له أي للقمر أربعة أفلاك تدوير هو مركوز حامل خارج المركوز هو في ثخن مائل أي ذلك الحامل فيما بين سطحي فلك الموافق المركز مسمى بالمائل لميلان منطقته عن منطقة البروج يحيط به أي بذلك المائل فلك آخر موافق مركزه أيضاً لمركز العالم وله أربع حركات فللتدوير حركة إلى التوالي في نصف هو الأسفل وإلى خلافه في نصف هو الأعلى وللخارج حركة إلى التوالي وللآخرين أي المائل والجوزهر حركتان إلى خلاف التوالي
So it is summarized from what we mentioned that the moon has four spheres: an epicycle (tadwīr) in which it is embedded, a deferent (khārij) outside the center, which is within the thickness of an inclined (mā'il) sphere. That is, the deferent is between the two surfaces of a concentric sphere, called inclined due to the inclination of its region from the ecliptic. Surrounding that inclined sphere is another concentric sphere whose center is also the center of the world. And it has four motions: for the epicycle, a motion in the sequence of signs (tawālī) in the lower half and opposite in the upper half; for the deferent, a motion in the sequence of signs; and for the other two (the inclined and the jawzahar), two motions opposite to the sequence of signs.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage provides a concise summary of the moon's astronomical model involving four spheres. It begins with the epicycle (التدوير) which carries the moon itself. This epicycle is embedded in a deferent sphere (الخارج) which is eccentric. The deferent lies within the thickness of an inclined sphere (المائل), so called because its plane is inclined relative to the ecliptic. Surrounding the inclined sphere is a concentric sphere (الموافق المركز) centered on the earth. The four spheres have four distinct motions: the epicycle moves in the direction of the sequence of signs (to the east) in its lower half and opposite (to the west) in its upper half; the deferent moves entirely in the direction of the sequence; the inclined sphere and the jawzahar sphere each move opposite to the sequence (to the west). The enumeration is systematic, first listing the spheres, then assigning their motions.

الجوزهر
حركة إلى التوالي
حركة إلى خلاف التوالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All spheres move in the same direction (eastward).
Correction: The passage shows that the epicycle moves eastward in its lower half and westward in its upper half, and the inclined and jawzahar spheres move entirely westward.

Checkpoint

A) The epicycle and deferent
B) The inclined and jawzahar
C) Only the epicycle
D) Only the deferent

Key Takeaway
The moon's apparent motion is the result of four superimposed rotations from four different spheres.

First Longitudinal Difference: The Epicycle Effect
Explains the first of three longitudinal differences caused by epicycle motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the epicycle causes a longitudinal difference in the moon's position and why no difference occurs at apogee or perigee.

Core Idea
The epicycle creates a longitudinal difference except at apogee and perigee.

This lesson explores the first of three longitudinal differences affecting the moon's position in the Ptolemaic model. The key factor is the epicycle, a small circle that carries the moon. When the moon is at the highest or lowest point of this circle, no angular difference arises; otherwise, an angle forms at the center of the world, which must be accounted for.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the terminology for apogee (الذروة) and perigee (الحضيض).
The passage is technical but geometrical.
2
Understand the two cases
Identify the two scenarios: (1) moon at apogee or perigee → lines coincide, no difference. (2) moon elsewhere → lines diverge, angle forms.
3
Visualize the geometry
Imagine the earth at center, epicycle center on a line, moon on epicycle. See how the two lines (to epicycle center and to moon) differ except at two points.
Drawing may help.
4
Connect to calculation
The angle formed is the 'difference' that sometimes needs to be subtracted. The passage ends with 'so it sometimes needs to be subtracted'.
This adjustment leads to true position.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you know when no difference occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله وللقمر في الطول وهو ما بين المغرب والمشرق اختلافات ثلاثة فأحدها هو الاختلاف الذي يكون بسبب التدوير فإن القمر إذا كان على ذروة التدوير أو حضيضه كان الخط الخارج من مركز العالم المار بمركز التدوير المنتهي إلى سطح الفلك الأعلى منطبقاً على الخط الخارج عنه المار بمركز القمر المنتهي إليه فلا اختلاف حينئذ بسببه. وإذا تحرك القمر بحركة التدوير نازلاً من الذروة أو صاعداً من الحضيض إلى جزء آخر من التدوير لم ينطبق أحد الخطين على الآخر بل حصل فيما بينها زاوية على مركز العالم فهذه الزاوية هي الاختلاف الناشئ من التدوير فيحتاج تارةً إلى أن تنقص
And the moon has three differences in longitude (ṭūl), which is between west and east. One of them is the difference that occurs due to the epicycle. For when the moon is at the apogee (dharwa) or perigee (ḥaḍīḍ) of the epicycle, the line drawn from the center of the world passing through the center of the epicycle and ending at the surface of the highest sphere coincides with the line drawn from it passing through the center of the moon and ending there, so there is no difference at that time due to it. And when the moon moves by the epicyclic motion descending from the apogee or ascending from the perigee to another part of the epicycle, the two lines do not coincide, but an angle occurs between them at the center of the world. This angle is the difference arising from the epicycle, so it sometimes needs to be subtracted...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage explains the first of three longitudinal (الطول) differences affecting the moon's position. It focuses on the difference caused by the epicycle (التدوير). When the moon is exactly at the apogee (ذروة) or perigee (حضيض) of the epicycle, the line from the earth's center through the epicycle center to the highest sphere coincides with the line through the moon itself, so no angular difference arises. But as the moon moves along the epicycle away from those two points, the two lines diverge, forming an angle at the earth's center. That angle is the longitudinal difference produced by the epicycle. The passage ends by noting that this angle sometimes needs to be subtracted (presumably from the mean motion to get the true position). The explanation is geometrical and quantitative, typical of Ptolemaic astronomy adopted in Islamic kalam.

Key Terms

التدوير
الذروة
الحضيض
الاختلاف
الخط الخارج من مركز العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The epicycle always causes a longitudinal difference in the moon's position.
Correction: The epicycle causes no difference when the moon is exactly at the apogee or perigee; only away from these points does an angle appear.

Checkpoint
When does the epicycle cause no longitudinal difference?
A) When the moon is at the nodes
B) When the moon is at apogee or perigee
C) When the moon is at quadrature
D) When the moon is at the midpoint

Key Takeaway
The epicycle causes a longitudinal difference except at the apogee and perigee, where the lines coincide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The epicycle causes a difference only when the moon departs from its highest or lowest point. Reflect on your own spiritual state: Are you at the 'height' of your connection to Allah, or have you drifted away, creating a 'difference' in your actions? What one change can you make today to realign?

The passage notes that the angle of difference sometimes needs to be subtracted. Think of a habit or attitude in your life that creates a 'difference' in your faith. Is it something you need to subtract (remove) to correct your course? Be specific about what that subtraction looks like.

The Moon's Fixed Latitudinal Width and Its Symmetric Variation
Al-Iji defines the fixed latitudinal limit and the rule of increase and decrease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fixed limit of the moon's latitudinal deviation and the symmetric pattern of its increase and decrease relative to the nodes and midpoints.

Core Idea
The moon's latitude is fixed at 5° and varies symmetrically around the ecliptic.

In this passage, al-Iji presents key observational facts about the moon's latitudinal motion. He establishes that the maximum latitude is constant at five degrees, and then describes how the latitude increases after passing the nodes and decreases after passing the midpoints, with a uniform proportion that ensures symmetry between the northern and southern arcs. This understanding is crucial for accurate astronomical calculations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage once to get an overview of the concepts. Pay attention to the terms for head and tail nodes (ra's and dhanab).
The parentheses in the Arabic are part of the commentary embedded in the text.
2
Identify the fixed limit
Note that al-Iji states the limit is five degrees on both north and south sides, and it does not change. This is the maximum latitudinal width.
3
Understand increase and decrease
Learn that latitude increases after crossing the nodes (the two intersection points with the ecliptic) and decreases after crossing the midpoints. Both follow the same proportion.
4
Observe the symmetry
Notice the symmetry: the increasing northern latitude at a given part from the head equals the southern counterpart from the tail. Similarly for decreasing with midpoints.
This symmetry makes the moon's latitudinal motion regular and predictable.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this uniform rule allows astronomers to compute the moon's latitude at any point in its orbit using simple arithmetic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغاية العرض في الجانبين) أي الشمال والجنوب (سواء ثابت لا يزيد ولا ينقص ومقدارها كما علم بالرصد خمسة أجزاء ) والتزايد) في العرض بعد مجاوزة العقدتين ( والتناقص ) فيه بعد مجاوزة المنتصفين ) بنسبة واحدة فهي ) أي العروض المتزايدة والمتناقصة ( متساوية في الأجزاء المتقابلة فالعرض المتزايد الشمالي للجزء العاشر من الرأس مثلاً يساوي العرض المتزايد الجنوبي للعاشر من الذنب . وكذا العرض المتناقض الشمالي للجزء الخامس من منتصف النصف الشمالي يساوي العرض المتناقص الجنوبي للجزء الخامس من المنتصف الآخر.
And the limit of latitude in both sides—i.e., north and south—is uniform, fixed, not increasing or decreasing, and its measure as known by observation is five degrees. And the increase in latitude after passing the two nodes, and the decrease in it after passing the two midpoints, are in a single proportion. So they—i.e., the increasing and decreasing latitudes—are equal in corresponding parts: the northern increasing latitude for the tenth part from the head, for example, equals the southern increasing latitude for the tenth part from the tail. Likewise, the northern decreasing latitude for the fifth part from the midpoint of the northern half equals the southern decreasing latitude for the fifth part from the other midpoint.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines the fixed limit (ghāyat al-ʿarḍ) of the moon's latitudinal deviation north and south of the ecliptic. The author states that this limit is constant at five degrees, determined by observation. He then describes the pattern of increase and decrease: the moon's latitude increases after crossing the nodes (the two points where the moon's orbit intersects the ecliptic) and decreases after crossing the midpoints (the points halfway between the nodes). The rate of change is uniform, so that the increasing latitude on the north side at a given distance from a node equals the increasing latitude on the south side at the corresponding distance from the opposite node. The same holds for decreasing latitude relative to the midpoints. This symmetry ensures that the moon's latitudinal motion is regular and predictable.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fixed limit of 5 degrees might be thought to apply to the moon's total variation in all directions, but it refers specifically to the maximum latitudinal deviation from the ecliptic.
Correction: The text explicitly states that the limit is the maximum latitude north or south, not the total range.

Key Takeaway
