The Moon's Four Spheres and Their Motions
Al-Iji enumerates the four spheres of the moon and their motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four spheres of the moon in Ptolemaic astronomy and the four types of motion that explain its complex path.

Core Idea
The moon's motion is governed by four nested spheres, each with a specific role.

This passage from al-Iji's Mawqif summarizes the Ptolemaic lunar model. Before we look at the details, note that the moon's motion is more complex than the sun's because of its varying speed and latitude. The model uses four spheres and four motions to account for these variations. Let us read the passage and then unpack each component.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own enumeration come through before you interpret.
Notice how al-Iji uses a very compressed listing style.
2
Identify the four spheres
The passage lists four spheres: the epicycle, the deferent (eccentric), the inclined sphere, and the concentric sphere. Find each one and note their relationship.
The inclined sphere is also called 'thick' because it has thickness.
3
Identify the four motions
There are four motions: epicycle has two (direct in lower half, retrograde in upper half), deferent one direct, and the inclined and node each retrograde. Count them in the text.
4
Understand the purpose
Ask yourself why this complex system is needed. The commentary explains that it accounts for the moon's variations in longitude and latitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد تلخص مما ذكرناه أن (له أي للقمر ( أربعة أفلاك تدوير هو مركوز ( حامل ) خارج المركوز ( هو في ثخن مائل ) أي ذلك الحامل فيما بين سطحي فلك الموافق المركز مسمى بالمائل لميلان منطقته عن منطقة البروج ( يحيط به أي بذلك المائل فلك آخر (موافق) مركزه أيضاً لمركز العالم ( وله أربع حركات فللتدوير) حركة ( إلى التوالي في نصف ) هو الأسفل ( وإلى خلافه في نصف ) هو الأعلى ( وللخارج) حركة (إلى التوالي وللآخرين) أي المائل والجوزهر حركتان إلى خلاف التوالي
It has become evident from what we have mentioned that he—i.e., the moon—has four spheres: an epicycle which is embedded in a deferent, which is eccentric, which is in a thick inclined [sphere]—i.e., that deferent is between the surfaces of the concentric sphere, called inclined because its circle is inclined from the circle of the zodiac—surrounding that inclined [sphere] is another sphere, concentric, its center also being the center of the world. And it has four motions: for the epicycle, a motion in the direction of the signs in one half—the lower—and opposite to that in the other half—the upper; for the deferent, a motion in the direction of the signs; and for the other two—i.e., the inclined and the node—two motions opposite to the direction of the signs.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage summarizes the Ptolemaic model for the moon, consisting of four spheres and four motions. The author lists the spheres: (1) the epicycle (tadwīr) in which the moon itself is embedded; (2) the deferent (ḥāmil), which is eccentric (khārij al-markaz) and carries the epicycle; (3) the inclined sphere (mā'il) which is a thick sphere between the concentric sphere and contains the deferent, its plane inclined relative to the zodiac; (4) a concentric sphere (muwāfiq) surrounding the inclined sphere, with its center at the center of the world. The motions are: the epicycle has two motions—one in the order of the signs (direct) in its lower half and opposite in its upper half; the deferent has one direct motion; and the inclined sphere and the node (jawzahar) each have a motion opposite to the order of the signs. This model explains the moon's complex longitudinal and latitudinal variations.

Key Terms
فلك
تدوير
حامل
خارج المركز
مائل
موافق
جوزهر
توالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'node' (جوزهر) is often misunderstood as the point of intersection of the moon's orbit with the ecliptic.
Correction: In this context, 'node' refers to a sphere (the nodical sphere) that accounts for the regression of the nodes, not the point itself.

Checkpoint

B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The Ptolemaic lunar model uses four nested spheres and four motions to explain the moon's complex longitudinal and latitudinal variations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The inclined sphere is tilted relative to the zodiac. Your life too has an 'inclination'—towards obedience or disobedience. As the spheres are precisely arranged by Allah, are you arranging your life's motions according to His design? What one tilt can you adjust today to align with His will?
?
The epicycle's motion is direct in one half and retrograde in the other. Reflect on times in your life when you moved 'directly' toward Allah and times when you moved away. Are you aware of the pattern and can you take a concrete step to stay in the direct half?
?
The node (jawzahar) regulates eclipses—when the moon's light is hidden. What 'eclipses' in your life hide the light of guidance? How does the knowledge of this precise mechanism encourage you to seek the light even in apparent darkness, trusting the One who set these motions?

The First Two Lunar Longitudinal Anomalies
Al-Iji enumerates and explains the first two longitudinal anomalies of the moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two anomalies in the moon's longitudinal motion: the epicycle anomaly and the deferent anomaly, and how they affect the moon's true position.

Core Idea
The moon's true motion differs from its mean motion due to epicycle and deferent effects.

Al-Iji introduces three longitudinal differences for the moon and details the first two. These anomalies are fundamental in Ptolemaic astronomy to compute the moon's true longitude. The first arises from the moon's motion on the epicycle, and the second from the deferent's eccentricity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the technical terms.
The passage is incomplete but covers the first two anomalies.
2
Understand the epicycle anomaly
Identify when the difference is zero (at apogee/perigee) and when it appears (elsewhere). Note that the angle must be added or subtracted to the mean motion.
The maximum difference equals the epicycle radius.
3
Understand the deferent anomaly
Grasp the alignment of the epicycle's diameter at deferent apogee/perigee and the misalignment elsewhere.
The passage ends mid-explanation; the key concept is the loss of alignment.
4
Connect the two anomalies
See how both anomalies combine to produce the moon's true longitude from the mean motion.
5
Reflect on the mean vs. true motion
The mean motion is of the epicycle center; the true motion is of the moon's own center.
This distinction is often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله وللقمر ( في الطول ( وهو ما بين المغرب والمشرق ( اختلافات ثلاثة ) فأحدها هو الاختلاف ( الذي ) يكون ( بسبب التدوير) فإن القمر إذا كان على ذروة التدوير أو حضيضه كان الخط الخارج من مركز العالم المار بمركز التدوير المنتهي إلى سطح الفلك الأعلى منطبقاً على الخط الخارج عنه المار بمركز القمر المنتهي إليه فلا اختلاف حينئذ بسببه . وإذا تحرك القمر بحركة التدوير نازلاً من الذروة أو صاعداً من الحضيض إلى جزء آخر من التدوير لم ينطبق أحد الخطين على الآخر بل حصل فيما بينها زاوية على مركز العالم فهذه الزاوية هي الاختلاف الناشئ من التدوير فيحتاج تارةً إلى أن تنقص هذه الزاوية عن وسط القمر. أعني حركة مركز تدويره وتارة إلى أن تزاد عليه حتى يتحصل تقويمه أعني حركة مركز نفسه. وغاية هذا الاختلاف هو نصف قطر التدوير. (و) ثانيها الاختلاف (الذي يكون ( بسبب الخارج) فإن مركز التدوير إذا كان في الأوج أو الحضيض كان قطر منه بعينه منطبقاً على الخط المار بمركز العالم والخارج والتدوير وبالأوج والحضيض والطرف الأعلى من هذا القطر هو ذروة التدوير التي هي مبدأ حركته الخاصة والطرف الآخر منه حضيضه المقابل لها فهما محاذيان في الحالتين لمركز العالم ومركز الخارج أيضاً . وإذا فارق مركز التدوير الأوج والحضيض لم يكن ذلك القطر منه منطبقاً على الخط الخارج من مركز العالم إلى مركز التدوير واصلا إلى أعلاه ولا على الخط الخارج من مركز الخارج إلى مركز التدوير كذلك فلا تكون الذورة المذكورة ومقابلها محاذيين لشيء من مركزي العالم والخارج. بل هما
And for him—i.e., the moon—in longitude—which is between west and east—there are three differences. The first of them is the difference that is due to the epicycle. For if the moon is at the apogee of the epicycle or its perigee, the line drawn from the center of the world passing through the center of the epicycle and ending at the surface of the highest sphere coincides with the line drawn from it passing through the center of the moon and ending at that surface; so there is no difference then due to it. But if the moon moves by the motion of the epicycle descending from the apogee or ascending from the perigee to another part of the epicycle, the two lines do not coincide; rather, an angle occurs between them at the center of the world. This angle is the difference arising from the epicycle. Sometimes this angle needs to be subtracted from the mean [motion] of the moon—I mean the motion of the center of its epicycle—and sometimes it needs to be added to it, so that its equation is obtained—I mean the motion of its own center. The maximum of this difference is the radius of the epicycle. And the second of them is the difference that is due to the deferent. For if the center of the epicycle is at the apogee or perigee, a particular diameter of it coincides with the line passing through the center of the world, the deferent, the epicycle, and the apogee and perigee; the upper end of this diameter is the apogee of the epicycle which is the beginning of its proper motion, and the other end is its perigee opposite to it. So they are aligned in both cases with the center of the world and also with the center of the deferent. And if the center of the epicycle departs from the apogee and perigee, that diameter of it does not coincide with the line from the center of the world to the center of the epicycle reaching its top, nor with the line from the center of the deferent to the center of the epicycle likewise. So the mentioned apogee and its opposite are not aligned with either of the centers of the world and the deferent. Rather, they are...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage classifies the three longitudinal anomalies of the moon (ikhtilāfāt thalātha) and details the first two. The first anomaly arises from the epicycle: when the moon is at the apogee or perigee of the epicycle, the line from the center of the world through the epicycle center to the highest sphere coincides with the line through the moon's center, so no angular difference. When the moon moves away, an angle appears at the world's center; this angle must be added to or subtracted from the mean motion (center of epicycle) to get the true motion (center of moon). The maximum value of this angle equals the epicycle's radius. The second anomaly arises from the deferent: when the epicycle center is at the deferent's apogee or perigee, the epicycle's diameter aligns with the line through world center, deferent center, and the apsides, so the epicycle's apogee and perigee are aligned. When the epicycle center moves away, the diameter no longer aligns, so the epicycle's apogee and perigee are not aligned with either center. The passage does not complete the explanation, nor does it mention the third anomaly (likely due to the inclined sphere and node).

Key Terms
اختلاف
طول
تدوير
خارج
ذروة
حضيض
أوج
وسط القمر
تقويم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mean motion refers to the moon's own motion.
Correction: The mean motion (وسط القمر) is the motion of the epicycle center, not the moon itself. The true motion (تقويم) is the motion of the moon's own center.

Checkpoint
What happens to the epicycle-induced longitudinal difference when the moon is at the apogee or perigee of the epicycle?
A) It is maximum
B) It is zero
C) It is half the radius
D) It is unpredictable

Key Takeaway
The moon's longitudinal motion involves two distinct anomalies: one from the epicycle and one from the deferent, each requiring correction to obtain the true position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The moon's true position requires correction of its mean motion. In your own life, what is your 'mean motion'—the routine that seems regular—that might actually need correction to align with your true purpose? Will you identify one specific area today and take a first step toward correction?
?
When the moon is aligned at its apogee, there is no anomaly. Are there moments in your faith where you feel perfectly aligned with Allah? How do you prevent the inevitable 'movement away' from creating a persistent angle of distance, and what does regular 'correction' look like for you?

The Epicycle Center of Venus: Northward Motion
Describes Venus's epicycle center motion relative to the ecliptic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the center of Venus's epicycle oscillates relative to the ecliptic, always remaining on or north of it.

Core Idea
Venus's epicycle center dances north of the ecliptic, never crossing south.

This passage describes the intricate motion of Venus's epicycle center as it travels along its orbit. Unlike the moon, which has a fixed latitude, Venus's epicycle center alternates between being on the ecliptic and being north of it. Understanding this motion is crucial for the Ptolemaic model of planetary latitudes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about the parentheses. Then read the translation to grasp the overall movement.
Notice that the passage uses terms like 'head' and 'tail' for nodes.
2
Follow the sequential description
Trace the motion from the head (on ecliptic) to the maximum latitude, back to the tail, then to the opposite half. Note how the halves switch roles.
The maximum latitude is the midpoint between the nodes.
3
Identify the conclusion
The passage concludes that the center is always either on the ecliptic or north of it. This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كأن الصفين) من مداري مركزي تدويرهما ( يتبادلان) في جهتي الشمال والجنوب ) فإذا كانت الزهرة بل مركز تدويرها ( على الرأس كان مدارها منطبقاً على سطح منطقة البروج ثم إذا جاوزت) الرأس ) وحصل) الكواكب بل مركز تدويره ( في النصف ) الذي يتحرك عليه (صار ذلك النصف شمالياً ) عن المنطقة والنصف الآخر جنوبياً عنها . ( ويتباعد ) المدار ( عنها شيئاً فشيئاً إلى أن يصل مركز تدويرها ( إلى غاية العرض ) وهي منتصف ما بين العقدتين (ثم يقرب مدارها (منها ) شيئاً فشيئاً (حتى ينطبق عليها وهي) أي الزهرة بل مركز تدويرها ( في الذنب ثم تصير في النصف الآخر) الذي كان جنوبياً ( وقد صار (هو) الآن ( شمالياً (و) صار النصف (الآخر) الذي قد تحرك عليه في الشمال جنوبياً ويتباعد ( المدار عنها في الجانبين ) إلى غاية ما هي منتصف هذا النصف ( ثم يتقارب إليها حتى ينطبق عليها ويتناول نصفاه في الجهة وهكذا دائماً . فيكون مركز تدوير الزهرة دائماً إما على المنطقة وإما في الشمال عنها . ( وأما .
قوله : ( كما ثبت للقمر أي كما ثبت عرض القمر وهو خمسة أجزاء كما مر، وثبوت عرض القمر هو أن يكون ثابتاً في جانب الشمال والجنوب لا يتبدل حاله فيهما بخلاف عرض الزهرة وعطارد وعن منطقة البروج كما ذكره .
قوله : ( وأما عرض مركز (جرميهما يعني أن المصنف لم يفرق بين عرض مركزهما وبين عرض أنفسهما . فعليك أن تفرق بين العرضين كيلا يشتبه عليك الحال . قوله : ( وهو منتصف ما بين (العقدتين وهذا المنتصف يكون في جانب الشمال. كما أن هذا النصف صار شمالياً عن المنطقة. وقوله : ثم تصير أي الزهرة بل مركز تدويرها. وقوله : وقد صار إلخ أي أن النصف الذي كان جنوبياً قد صار هو الآن شمالياً .
قوله : ( فيكون مركز تدوير الزهرة دائماً ) ولعل سبب هذه الحالة هو حركة الحامل منضمة إلى اعتبار بعد مركز الحامل عن مركز فلك البروج والاعتبار هاهنا لتحريك التدوير مركز نفس الكوكب إذ الكلام في حركة الكوكب لا في حركة مركز الكوكب حتى يعتبر تحريك التدوير
عطارد فبالعكس من ذلك فيكون عند الانطباق في الذنب ويتجاوزه إلى النصف الجنوبي متباعداً ثم ينطبق وهو يتجاوزه إلى النصف الآخر)، الذي كان شمالياً ( وقد صار الآن (جنوبیاً ) فمركز تدویره دائماً إما على المنطقة وإما في الجنوب عنها،
(It is) as if the two lines) from the orbits of the centers of their two epicycles (alternate) in the direction of north and south. When Venus, or rather the center of its epicycle, (is at the head, its orbit coincides with the surface of the ecliptic; then when it passes) the head (and) the planet, or rather the center of its epicycle, (enters the half) upon which it moves, (that half becomes northern) relative to the ecliptic and the other half southern relative to it. (And the orbit (moves away) from it little by little until the center of its epicycle reaches (the limit of latitude), which is the midpoint between the two nodes; (then its orbit approaches) (it) little by little (until it coincides with it, and it), i.e. Venus with the center of its epicycle, (is at the tail; then it enters the other half), which was southern, (and it has now become) northern, (and) the (other) half, upon which it had moved in the north, becomes southern; and the orbit (moves away) from it on both sides) to the limit which is the midpoint of this half; (then it approaches it until it coincides with it, and its two halves exchange in direction, and so on always. So the center of Venus's epicycle is always either on the ecliptic or north of it. (As for ).
His statement: (as established for the moon) i.e. as the latitude of the moon was established, which is five degrees as passed, and the establishment of the moon's latitude is that it is fixed on the side of north and south; its state does not change in them, unlike the latitude of Venus and Mercury and from the ecliptic as he mentioned.
His statement: (As for the latitude of the center of their two bodies) means that the author did not distinguish between the latitude of their center and the latitude of their bodies. So you must distinguish between the two latitudes so that the case may not be confused for you. His statement: (And it is the midpoint between the two nodes) and this midpoint is on the side of the north, just as this half became northern relative to the ecliptic. And his statement: 'then it enters' means Venus with the center of its epicycle. And his statement: 'and it has become' etc. means that the half which was southern has now become northern.
His statement: (So the center of Venus's epicycle is always) and perhaps the cause of this state is the motion of the carrier (al-hāmil) combined with taking into account the distance of the center of the carrier from the center of the sphere of the ecliptic; and the consideration here is for moving the epicycle the center of the planet itself, for the discussion is about the motion of the planet, not about the motion of the center of the planet such that the motion of the director (al-mudīr) would be considered.
Mercury is the opposite of that, so that when coinciding at the tail it passes beyond it to the southern half moving away, then it coincides as it passes beyond it to the other half), which was northern (and has now become southern). So the center of its epicycle is always either on the ecliptic or south of it.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a detailed astronomical description explaining the motion of the epicycle centers of Venus and Mercury relative to the ecliptic. It begins by comparing the orbits of the two epicycle centers to two lines that alternate north and south. For Venus, when its epicycle center is at the head (one of the nodes), its orbit lies on the ecliptic. As it moves from the head into the half in which it travels, that half becomes northern relative to the ecliptic and the other half southern. The orbit gradually moves away until the center reaches the maximum latitude (midpoint between nodes), then approaches until coinciding again when the center is at the tail. The process reverses as it enters the other half, with the two halves switching roles. Thus Venus's epicycle center is always either on the ecliptic or north of it. The commentator then clarifies a distinction: the latitude of the epicycle center is not the same as the latitude of the planet's body. He explains that the author (al-muṣannif) did not differentiate, but the commentator warns the reader to distinguish them. He also notes that the midpoint between the nodes is in the north. A possible cause for this behavior is given: the motion of the carrier sphere (al-ḥāmil) combined with the offset of its center from the ecliptic center, and the discussion here concerns the motion of the planet itself, not the center of the planet (which would involve the director sphere). Finally, Mercury behaves oppositely: its epicycle center is always on the ecliptic or south of it.

منطقة البروج
العقدتان (الرأس والذنب)
غاية العرض

Key Takeaway
Venus's epicycle center oscillates north of the ecliptic, always remaining on or north of it.

Latitude of Center vs. Body; Mercury's Opposite Motion
Distinguishes between two latitudes and describes Mercury's opposite epicycle center motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the latitude of the epicycle center and the latitude of the planet's body, and how Mercury's epicycle center moves opposite to Venus's.

Core Idea
Don't confuse the center's path with the planet's own latitude; Mercury swings south while Venus swings north.

The commentator warns us about a common confusion: the latitude of the epicycle center is not the same as the latitude of the planet itself. This lesson clarifies that distinction and then describes the opposite behavior of Mercury's epicycle center, which always remains on or south of the ecliptic.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's warning
Pay attention to the first part where the commentator distinguishes between the latitude of the center and the latitude of the bodies. He urges the reader not to confuse them.
The author (muṣannif) did not explicitly separate them, so the commentator does.
2
Understand the explanation of the midpoint and cause
The midpoint between the nodes is in the north. The cause of the motion is the carrier sphere combined with the offset of its center from the ecliptic center.
The discussion is specifically about the motion of the planet, not the motion of the center of the planet.
3
Compare with Mercury's behavior
Mercury is opposite: its epicycle center is always on the ecliptic or south of it. Read the description carefully to see the reversal.

(It is) as if the two lines) from the orbits of the centers of their two epicycles (alternate) in the direction of north and south. His statement: (And it is the midpoint between the two nodes) and this midpoint is on the side of the north, just as this half became northern relative to the ecliptic. And his statement: 'then it enters' means Venus with the center of its epicycle. And his statement: 'and it has become' etc. means that the half which was southern has now become northern.
His statement: (So the center of Venus's epicycle is always) and perhaps the cause of this state is the motion of the carrier (al-hāmil) combined with taking into account the distance of the center of the carrier from the center of the sphere of the ecliptic; and the consideration here is for moving the epicycle the center of the planet itself, for the discussion is about the motion of the planet, not about the motion of the center of the planet such that the motion of the director (al-mudīr) would be considered.
Mercury is the opposite of that, so that when coinciding at the tail it passes beyond it to the southern half moving away, then it coincides as it passes beyond it to the other half), which was northern (and has now become southern). So the center of its epicycle is always either on the ecliptic or south of it.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a detailed astronomical description explaining the motion of the epicycle centers of Venus and Mercury relative to the ecliptic. It begins by comparing the orbits of the two epicycle centers to two lines that alternate north and south. For Venus, when its epicycle center is at the head (one of the nodes), its orbit lies on the ecliptic. As it moves from the head into the half in which it travels, that half becomes northern relative to the ecliptic and the other half southern. The orbit gradually moves away until the center reaches the maximum latitude (midpoint between nodes), then approaches until coinciding again when the center is at the tail. The process reverses as it enters the other half, with the two halves switching roles. Thus Venus's epicycle center is always either on the ecliptic or north of it. The commentator then clarifies a distinction: the latitude of the epicycle center is not the same as the latitude of the planet's body. He explains that the author (al-muṣannif) did not differentiate, but the commentator warns the reader to distinguish them. He also notes that the midpoint between the nodes is in the north. A possible cause for this behavior is given: the motion of the carrier sphere (al-ḥāmil) combined with the offset of its center from the ecliptic center, and the discussion here concerns the motion of the planet itself, not the center of the planet (which would involve the director sphere). Finally, Mercury behaves oppositely: its epicycle center is always on the ecliptic or south of it.

Key Terms
عرض مركز التدوير
عرض الجرم
الحامل
المدير
العقدتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The latitude of the epicycle center is the same as the latitude of the planet's body.
Correction: The commentator explicitly warns that they are distinct. The center's latitude refers to the orbit of the epicycle, while the planet's body latitude includes additional effects from its motion on the epicycle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, where is the center of Mercury's epicycle always located?
A) Always north of the ecliptic
B) Always on the ecliptic
C) Either on the ecliptic or south of it
D) Either on the ecliptic or north of it

Key Takeaway
Mercury's epicycle center is always on the ecliptic or south of it, opposite to Venus, and the latitude of the center must be distinguished from the planet's body latitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator warns that the author did not distinguish between the latitude of the center and the body, and insists that you must distinguish. In your own understanding of your faith, where might you be confusing the 'center' (core essential beliefs) with the 'body' (details or peripherals)? What steps will you take to ensure you don't confuse them?
?
Mercury's epicycle center is always either on the ecliptic or south—never north. Think of a principle or value in your life that you have always assumed to be 'south' but might actually be 'north', or vice versa. Are you willing to re-examine your assumptions based on correct knowledge?
?
This lesson shows how a small confusion between two similar terms (center's latitude vs. body's latitude) can lead to misunderstanding the entire motion. Is there a relationship or teaching in your religious practice that you have oversimplified to the point of error? How will you seek clarity?

Two Additional Latitudes of Venus and Mercury
Explains two additional latitudes and the behavior of the first diameter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Venus and Mercury acquire two additional latitudes due to the inclination of the epicycle diameter that passes through apogee and perigee, and how the tilts of apogee and perigee differ for each planet.

Core Idea
The epicycle diameter's tilt relative to the ecliptic creates additional north-south latitudes for Venus and Mercury.

This lesson explores the second type of latitude for the inferior planets. Building on the latitude of the epicycle center, the author explains that the diameter through apogee and perigee of their epicycles does not always lie in the ecliptic plane; its inclination gives rise to additional displacements north or south. We will examine how this works and why Venus and Mercury behave oppositely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two additional latitudes
Focus on the opening statement that Venus and Mercury have two other latitudes different from the first. Note the cause is the orbit of the epicycle center.
The passage includes commentary that clarifies terms.
2
Understand the behavior of the diameter through apogee and perigee
The passage explains that this diameter only coincides with the ecliptic at the midpoint between nodes. At the nodes it is maximally inclined.
This is key to the additional latitudes.
3
Study the tilts for Venus and Mercury
The commentary details how the apogee and perigee tilt north/south depending on whether the epicycle center is descending or ascending. Notice the opposite pattern for Venus and Mercury.
This explains the name 'additional latitudes'.
4
Synthesize the additional latitude effect
Reflect on how these tilts produce additional north-south displacements beyond the center's latitude, giving each planet its characteristic pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لهما أي للزهرة وعطارد ) عرضان آخران مغايران لعرضهما بسبب مدار مرکز تدويرهما على الوجه الذي صورناه ) فإن القطر من تدويرهما (المار بالذروة والحضيض ينطبق تارة على المنطقة وينفصل عنه أخرى ( كأنه أراد بالمنطقة مدار مركز تدويرهما فإن انطباق ذلك القطر إنما هو عليه في منتصف ما بین العقد تين. دون منطقة البروج في العقدتين إذ هو هناك في غاية الميل عن المدار ومنطقة البروج المتطابقين ولذلك أمكن لجرم الزهرة عرض جنوبي ولجرم عطارد عرض شمالي كما أشرنا إليه. وكذلك القطر المار بالبعدين الأوسطين من تدويرهما المقاطع للقطر المار
أيضاً. ولا اعتبار هاهنا أيضاً لتحريك المدير مركز الحامل. فإن مثل هذه الحالة يكون في مركز تدوير عطارد وليس هناك محرك آخر مثل المدير حتى يعتبر تحريكه .
قوله بسبب مدار مرکز (تدويرهما متعلق بقوله : لعرضهما . لا بقوله : متغايران . وقوله : عليه أي على مدار مركز تدويرهما . وقوله : إذ هو أي ذلك القطر المار بالذروة والحضيض. وقوله : هناك أي في العقدتين. وقوله : في غاية الميل عن المدار إلخ، فالزهرة ما دام مركزها هابطاً من الأوج من أحد المنتصفين الشماليين مالت ذروتها إلى الشمال وحضيضها إلى الجنوب وما دام مركزها صاعداً من الحضيض من المنتصف الآخر مالت ذروتها إلى الجنوب وحضيضها إلى الشمال على عكس النصف الأول، وأما عطارد فمادام مركزه هابطا من الأوج مالت ذروته إلى الجنوب وحضيضه إلى الشمال، ومادام صاعداً من الحضيض مالت ذروته إلى الشمال وحضيضه
إلى الجنوب.
قوله : ( وكذلك القطر المار بالبعدين الأوسطين من تدويرهما البعدان الأوسطان هاهنا هما النقطتان المتقابلتان في محيط التدوير بحيث تكون كل واحدة منهما بالنسبة إلى مركز العالم ،سواء، وكذا يكون بعد كل واحدة منهما بالنسبة إلى مركز ذلك التدوير سواء كالقطر المار بالبعدين الأوسطين المقاطع للقطر المار بالذروة والحضيض على قوائم يكون له طرفان . أحدهما هو الطرف المتأخر أعني الطرف الشرقي ويقال له : البعد المسامتي، والآخر هو الطرف المتقدم أعني الطرف الغربي يقال له : البعد الصباحي إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا وصل مركز تدوير السفليين أعني الزهرة وعطارد إلى أحد العقدتين يكون هذا القطر المار بالبعدين الأوسطين منطبقاً على مدار مركز التدوير ومنطقة البروج، أي يكون هو في سطحهما حال كونهما متطابقين ويصير حينئذ أحد طرفي القطر المار بالذروة والحضيض إلى الشمال والطرف الآخر إلى
Then they, i.e. Venus and Mercury, have two other latitudes, different from their (first) latitude, because of the orbit of the center of their epicycle in the manner we have depicted. For the diameter of their epicycle (passing through apogee and perigee coincides sometimes with the (ecliptic) zone and separates from it at other times) — as if he meant by 'zone' the orbit of the center of their epicycle; for the coincidence of that diameter is only upon it at the midpoint between the two nodes, not on the ecliptic at the two nodes, for there it is at the extreme inclination from the orbit and the ecliptic which coincide. And therefore a southern latitude is possible for the body of Venus and a northern latitude for the body of Mercury, as we indicated. And likewise the diameter passing through the two mean distances of their epicycle, which intersects the diameter passing through (apogee and perigee), also (does the same). And there is no consideration here either for moving the director the center of the carrier. For such a state occurs in the center of Mercury's epicycle, and there is no other mover like the director so that its motion would be considered.
His statement: 'because of the orbit of the center (of their epicycle)' is attached to his statement: 'for their latitude', not to his statement: 'different'. And his statement: 'upon it' means upon the orbit of the center of their epicycle. And his statement: 'for it' means that diameter passing through apogee and perigee. And his statement: 'there' means at the two nodes. And his statement: 'at the extreme inclination from the orbit' etc., so Venus, as long as its center is descending from apogee from one of the two northern midpoints, its apogee tilts to the north and its perigee to the south; and as long as its center is ascending from perigee from the other midpoint, its apogee tilts to the south and its perigee to the north, opposite to the first half. As for Mercury, as long as its center is descending from apogee, its apogee tilts to the south and its perigee to the north; and as long as it is ascending from perigee, its apogee tilts to the north and its perigee to the south.
His statement: (And likewise the diameter passing through the two mean distances of their epicycle) — the two mean distances here are the two opposite points on the circumference of the epicycle such that each of them is equidistant relative to the center of the world, and likewise the distance of each of them relative to the center of that epicycle is equal; like the diameter passing through the two mean distances, intersecting the diameter passing through apogee and perigee at right angles; it has two ends: one is the trailing end, i.e. the eastern end, called the meridian distance (al-buʿd al-musāmītī), and the other is the leading end, i.e. the western end, called the morning distance (al-buʿd al-ṣubāḥī). If you understand this, we say: When the center of the epicycle of the two inferior planets, i.e. Venus and Mercury, reaches one of the two nodes, this diameter passing through the two mean distances coincides with the orbit of the center of the epicycle and the ecliptic, i.e. it lies in their plane while they coincide, and then one end of the diameter passing through apogee and perigee becomes (north and the other end becomes)
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage explains that Venus and Mercury have two additional latitudes beyond the latitude of their epicycle centers. These arise because the diameter of the epicycle that passes through apogee and perigee does not always lie on the ecliptic; it only coincides with the orbit of the epicycle center at the midpoint between the two nodes. At the nodes themselves, this diameter is maximally inclined relative to the ecliptic and the orbit (which coincide there). This inclination allows the bodies of Venus and Mercury to have a different latitude: Venus's body can have a southern latitude while Mercury's can have a northern one (in addition to the latitudes of their centers). The commentator then details the behavior: for Venus, when its epicycle center descends from apogee in one of the northern midpoints, the apogee of the epicycle tilts north and perigee south; when ascending from perigee in the other midpoint, apogee tilts south and perigee north. Mercury behaves oppositely. Next, the diameter through the two mean distances (the endpoints that are equidistant from the center of the world and from the epicycle center) is introduced; this diameter is perpendicular to the diameter through apogee and perigee. Its two ends are named: the trailing/eastern end (al-buʿd al-musāmītī) and the leading/western end (al-buʿd al-ṣubāḥī). When the epicycle center reaches a node, this diameter coincides with the orbit of the epicycle center and the ecliptic (which are aligned), and then the ends of the diameter through apogee and perigee point north-south.

Key Terms
القطر المار بالذروة والحضيض
العرضان الآخران
منتصف ما بين العقدتين
العقدتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two additional latitudes are the same as the latitude of the epicycle center.
Correction: The passage explicitly calls them 'different' (مغايران) because they arise from the tilt of the epicycle diameter, not from the center's orbit alone.

Checkpoint
At which point does the diameter through apogee and perigee coincide with the orbit of the epicycle center?
A) At the two nodes
B) At the midpoint between the two nodes
C) At the maximum latitude
D) At the apogee

Key Takeaway
The tilt of the epicycle's diameter through apogee and perigee relative to the ecliptic produces additional north-south latitudes for Venus and Mercury, with opposite behaviors for each planet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that Venus and Mercury have opposite tilts for their additional latitudes, reflecting a deliberate design. In your own life, are you acknowledging the deliberate design in creation, or do you take it for granted? What specific change can you make today to increase your gratitude for the precision of the universe?
?
The text mentions that at the nodes, the diameter is extremely inclined. This complexity is part of the celestial machinery. How does knowing this affect your trust in Allah's wisdom over all affairs, even those you don't understand? Can you name one area of your life where you need to submit to His intricate planning?

The Second Diameter and Node Alignment of Venus and Mercury
Defines the second diameter and its behavior at nodes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the diameter through the two mean distances of the epicycle, the names of its ends, and its alignment with the ecliptic when the epicycle center reaches a node.

Core Idea
The diameter through the mean distances, perpendicular to the apogee-perigee diameter, defines the east-west axis of the epicycle and aligns with the ecliptic at nodes.

This lesson continues with the geometry of the epicycle for Venus and Mercury. The author introduces the second important diameter—the one passing through the two mean distances. We will learn about its orientation, its ends named 'morning distance' and 'meridian distance', and what happens when the epicycle center reaches a node.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the two mean distances
The two mean distances are opposite points on the epicycle circumference that are equidistant from the center of the world and from the epicycle center.
The diameter connecting them is perpendicular to the apogee-perigee diameter.
2
Identify the two ends of this diameter
One end is the trailing/eastern end (al-buʿd al-musāmītī) and the other is the leading/western end (al-buʿd al-ṣubāḥī). Note their names.
These names relate to the planet's visibility.
3
Understand the configuration at a node
When the epicycle center reaches one of the nodes, this second diameter coincides with the orbit of the epicycle center and the ecliptic (which are aligned). Then the apogee-perigee diameter's ends point north-south.
This completes the description of the additional latitudes.
4
Connect to the overall latitude model
Reflect on how both diameters together produce the two additional latitudes: one set from the tilt of the apogee-perigee diameter and the other from the orientation at nodes.

Geometry of Mercury's Equant and Nodes
Geometric description of Mercury's equant and nodes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric relationship between the equant point, nodes, and diameters in the Ptolemaic model for Mercury, and why this creates inconsistencies with uniform circular motion.

Core Idea
Mercury's equant model contradicts uniform circular motion at its core.

This passage describes the detailed geometry of Mercury's epicycle center motion relative to the equant point. The author explains how two diameters (through mean distances and through apogee/perigee) intersect and rotate as the epicycle center moves between nodes. This geometric description highlights a fundamental problem in the Ptolemaic model: the assumption of uniform motion around the deferent is violated by the equant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the geometric flow. Then read the translation, noting the two diameters and the two positions (at node and at midpoint between nodes).
The passage includes marginal comments at the end; these are later clarifications.
2
Visualize the geometry
Draw a diagram: the orbit of the epicycle center, mark the nodes, and show the two diameters. Trace how they shift as the epicycle center moves from a node to the midpoint.
The key is that at the midpoint, one diameter becomes aligned north-south (node line) and the other is perpendicular but still lies on the orbit.
3
Understand the criticism through marginal comments
The marginal comments hint that the Ptolemaic model's principle of intersecting spheres with eccentricity fails to preserve uniform motion. Reflect on why the geometry described leads to a contradiction.
The commentator suggests that 'they did not speak' may imply they actually did speak, i.e., they acknowledged the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجنوب، وهذا غاية ميله عن مدار مركز التدوير. وحينئذ يكون هذان القطران متقاطعين على زوايا قائمة ثم إذا فارق مركز التدوير عن إحدى العقدتين فأحد طرفي كل واحد من القطرين مال إلى الشمال والطرف الآخر مال إلى الجنوب حتى إذا وصل مركز التدوير إلى منتصف ما بين العقدتين صار أحد طرفي القطر المار بالبعدين الأوسطين في الشمال والآخر في الجنوب، وهذا المنطقة وصار القطر المار بالذروة والحضيض منتصباً على ذلك القطر ومتقاطعاً له على زوايا قوائم ومع ذلك صار منطبقاً على مدار مركز التدوير كما ذكر. قوله : (لم يتكلموا ) لعلهم كانوا يتكلمون أن ذلك المبدأ هو اجتماع الافلاك المخالفة مع انضمام اعتبار خروج مركزها عن مركز العالم. وقوله : ينحى بالقلع أي يقبل بالقلع.
The south, and this is the limit of its inclination from the orbit of the epicycle center. Then these two diameters intersect at right angles. Then when the epicycle center departs from one of the two nodes, one end of each diameter inclines to the north and the other end inclines to the south, until when the epicycle center reaches the midpoint between the two nodes, one end of the diameter passing through the two mean distances becomes in the north and the other in the south, and this is the node; and the diameter passing through the apogee and perigee becomes perpendicular to that diameter and intersecting it at right angles, and yet it becomes coincident with the orbit of the epicycle center as mentioned. His saying: 'They did not speak'—perhaps they used to say that that principle is the conjunction of the intersecting spheres together with the consideration of the eccentricity of their centers from the center of the world. And his saying: 'he demolishes' meaning he destroys.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage provides a geometric description of the motion of Mercury's epicycle center in relation to the equant point and nodes. The commentator explains: when the epicycle center is at its extreme southern inclination, two diameters (one through the mean distances, one through apogee and perigee) intersect at right angles. As the epicycle center moves away from a node, the ends of these diameters shift north and south. At the midpoint between nodes, the diameter through the mean distances becomes aligned north-south (this is the node line), while the diameter through apogee and perigee becomes perpendicular to it, yet still lies on the epicycle's orbit. This geometric configuration illustrates why the Ptolemaic model fails: it assumes uniform motion around the deferent center, but the equant introduces a different center, making the motion non-uniform relative to the deferent. The final two lines are marginal comments: the first suggests that 'they did not speak' (lam yanbisū) might mean they actually did speak, attributing the cause to intersecting spheres and eccentricities; the second clarifies that 'yanḥā bi-l-qalʿ' means 'he destroys' or 'he uproots' (i.e., demolishes the foundation).

Key Terms
عقدة (ʿuqda, node)
ذروة (dharwa, apogee)
حضيض (ḥaḍīḍ, perigee)
قطر (quṭr, diameter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage is an endorsement of the Ptolemaic model.
Correction: This is not an endorsement but a detailed explanation of why the model fails by its own geometric constraints.

Checkpoint
What geometric condition is described when the epicycle center reaches the midpoint between two nodes?
A) The two diameters become parallel.
B) One diameter becomes perpendicular to the other and both lie on the epicycle orbit.
C) The epicycle center stops moving.
D) The diameters tilt to the east and west.

Key Takeaway
The geometry of Mercury's equant shows that uniform motion around the deferent is impossible, revealing a flaw in the Ptolemaic system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The equant model was an attempt to 'save the appearances' despite internal inconsistency. What would it cost you to examine it honestly, and are you willing to pay that price?

The commentator demolishes (yanḥā bi-l-qalʿ) a well-established theory when its flaws are exposed. Is there a long-held assumption in your life—about yourself, your faith, or your worldview—that you are afraid to see demolished? Write down one such assumption and commit to testing it against the truth, even if it unsettles you.
?
This lesson shows that even in a precise geometric system, contradictions can be hidden. When you discover a contradiction in your own thinking, do you tend to rationalize it away or let it drive you to deeper inquiry? Reflect on a specific instance and resolve this week to pursue truth over comfort.

How the Moon's Phases Occur: From Crescent to Full Moon and Back
Geometrical explanation of the moon's phases from conjunction to opposition and back.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometrical explanation for the moon's phases, including why it appears as a crescent, full moon, and disappears (new moon), based on the illumination from the sun and the relative positions of the sun, moon, and Earth.

Core Idea
The moon's phases are caused by the changing angle of its illuminated half relative to Earth.

This passage from a classical kalam work provides a strikingly modern geometrical explanation of the lunar phases. It describes how the moon's illumination from the sun creates a visible crescent that waxes to a full moon and then wanes. The author uses the concepts of the 'circle of vision' and 'circle of light' to explain why we see different shapes. Let's explore this fascinating account.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description of the phases come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of circles to describe visibility.
2
Understand the initial condition at conjunction
The passage begins with the moon in conjunction with the sun. The illuminated side faces away from Earth, so the moon is invisible. The circles of vision and light coincide.
3
Trace the waxing from crescent to full
As the moon moves away from the sun, the circles intersect, revealing a thin arc (crescent). The visible part grows until the entire illuminated face is toward Earth at opposition (full moon).
The author describes the process in detail.
4
Follow the waning from full to new moon
After opposition, the moon approaches from the other side, the circles intersect again, and the illuminated face gradually turns away, shrinking to a crescent and then disappearing (new moon).
This completes the cycle.
5
Examine the reason for invisibility after conjunction
The passage explains why the moon is not seen for a day or more: weak light, thin crescent, and proximity to the sun's overwhelming brightness.
Visibility also depends on other factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثاني : المقصد الأول : القمر واستضاءته من الشمس
في الهلال والبدر القمر لما كان يستضيء من الشمس فنصفه المقابل لها أبداً مضيء ونصفه الآخر مظلم، ولما كان تحت الشمس فإذا كان القمر مقارناً لها كان وجهه المضيء إليها دوننا فلا نرى له ضوءاً أصلاً ( وكانت) حينئذ ( دائرة الرؤية) وهي الدائرة الفاصلة بين المرئي وغير المرئي منه ( منطبقة على دائرة الضوء وهي ) الدائرة ( الفاصلة بين المضيء والمظلم منه ونفرض دائرة الرؤية ثابتة و ) نقول : ( دائرة الضوء تزول ) لا محالة ( بزواله ) أي تزول بزوال القمر ) عن المسامتة ) أي المقارنة للشمس ) فبعد الانفراج بينهما ) أي بعد زوال الانطباق وحصول الانكشاف والتقاطع بين الدائرتين يقع شيء من الوجه المضيء مستدق بين نصفيهما وحينئذ ( نرى قوسا من الوجه المضيء إلينا فهذا المرئي هو الهلال ) ولا يزال ذلك يكبر بالبعد عن الشمس ويزداد المرئي من الوجه المضيء عظماً ) حتى يصير الوجه المضيء) بتمامه ( إلينا ) وذلك عند المقابلة التي هي غاية البعد بينهما ( و ) حينئذ ( ينطبق الدائرتان مرة أخرى فنراه (بدراً كاملاً كدائرة تامة ( ثم) إن النيرين بعد غاية البعد بينهما ( يتقاربان ) من الجانب الآخر ( فتتقاطع ) حينئذ ( الدائرتان ) مرة أخرى ( وينحرف عنا شيء مستدق من الوجه ( المضيء ) فينتقص كمال البدرية، وهكذا ينحرف المضيء شيئاً فشيئاً (حتى ترى منه شكلاً هلالياً في جانب المشرق ثم ( يخفى بالكلية وهو المحاق وإنما لا نرى) القمر ( يوماً وأكثر بعد المقارنة وقبلها لضعف ضوئه ودقته وقربه من الشمس مع ضوئها ) الغالب الساتر لما يقرب منها (فيمتنع) القمر لهذه الأسباب ) من إبصاره) وأما إذا كان بعيداً عنها في أحد جانبيها بمقدار اثنتي عشرة درجة فإنه يرى عادة مستمرة وربما نرى بأقل منها فإن ذلك مما يختلف بحسب عرض القمر وصفاء الأفق وقوة الباصرة .
First Observatory - Second Section: First Objective: The Moon and Its Illumination from the Sun. Concerning the crescent and the full moon: Since the moon receives light from the sun, its half facing the sun is always illuminated and its other half is dark. And since it is below the sun, when the moon is in conjunction with the sun, its illuminated side faces the sun away from us, so we see no light at all. Then (the circle of vision)—which is the circle separating the visible from the invisible part of it—(coincides with the circle of light)—which is the circle separating the illuminated from the dark part. We assume the circle of vision is fixed, and we say: the circle of light necessarily disappears with its disappearance—i.e., it disappears with the moon's departure from alignment—i.e., from conjunction with the sun. So after the separation between them—i.e., after the coincidence disappears and the two circles intersect—a thin part of the illuminated face lies between the two halves, and then (we see an arc of the illuminated face toward us, and this visible part is the crescent). It continues to increase with distance from the sun, and the visible part of the illuminated face grows larger until the entire illuminated face (is toward us), and that occurs at opposition, which is the greatest distance between them. Then the two circles coincide again, and we see it as a perfect full moon, like a complete circle. Then, after the greatest distance between them, the two luminaries approach from the other side, so the two circles intersect again, and a thin part of the illuminated face turns away from us, so the perfection of the fullness decreases. Thus the illuminated part gradually turns away until a crescent shape is seen from it on the eastern side, then it disappears completely, and that is the new moon. And we do not see the moon for a day or more after conjunction and before it due to the weakness and fineness of its light and its proximity to the sun with its overwhelming light that covers what is near it. So the moon is prevented from being seen for these reasons. But if it is distant from it on one side by about twelve degrees, it is usually seen continuously, and we may see it at less than that, for that varies according to the moon's latitude, the clarity of the horizon, and the strength of the eyesight.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed geometrical explanation of the moon's phases. It begins by stating the basic principle: the half of the moon facing the sun is always illuminated. Then it describes the situation at conjunction (new moon): the illuminated side faces the sun away from Earth, so no light reaches us; the circle of vision (the dividing line between what we see of the moon and what we don't) coincides with the circle of light (the dividing line between the illuminated and dark halves). As the moon moves away from the sun, the circles intersect, revealing a thin arc of the illuminated side—this is the crescent. As the distance increases, more of the illuminated face becomes visible until the moon is opposite the sun (full moon) and the entire illuminated face is toward us. Then as the moon approaches from the other side, the illuminated face turns away again, decreasing until only a crescent is visible in the east, then complete disappearance (new moon). The passage also explains why the moon is invisible for a day or more after conjunction: its light is weak, it is thin, and it is near the sun's overwhelming light. It ends by noting that visibility depends on the moon's latitude, horizon clarity, and eyesight strength. This is a clear scientific explanation within a kalam work.

Key Terms
الهلال
البدر
المحاق
مقارنة
مقابلة
دائرة الرؤية
دائرة الضوء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moon is invisible during new moon because it is completely dark.
Correction: The moon is not completely dark; its illuminated side faces away from Earth, and its light is overwhelmed by the sun.

Checkpoint
Why is the moon invisible for a day or more after conjunction?
A) The moon is completely dark at that time.
B) The moon's illuminated side faces away from Earth and it is near the sun's overwhelming light.
C) The moon is covered by the Earth's shadow.
D) The moon moves too fast to be seen.

Key Takeaway
The moon's phases are explained by the geometry of illumination from the sun: at new moon the illuminated side faces away; at full moon it faces Earth; and in between we see portions of the illuminated half.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The moon is hidden after conjunction, yet it still receives light. In your life, when you feel unseen or unappreciated for your good deeds, will you remember that your 'illuminated side' remains directed toward Allah even if no one else sees? What is one act of worship you will perform this week solely for His sight, expecting no human recognition?
?
The crescent grows gradually from a thin arc to a full moon. Are your spiritual practices—prayer, charity, patience—growing steadily, or are you satisfied with a sliver of faith? Name one specific habit you will increase by a little each day this month to move toward fullness.
?
The passage says visibility also depends on the clarity of the horizon and strength of eyesight. What 'dust' on the horizon of your heart—sins, distractions, or attachments—is blurring your vision of Allah's signs? What concrete step will you take today to clear it so you can see His light more clearly?

The Crescent Moon Formation
Sanusi explains the geometric cause of the crescent moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the crescent moon first becomes visible through the intersection of the circle of vision and the circle of light.

Core Idea
The crescent appears when the circle of light and circle of vision intersect.

This lesson unpacks the astronomical geometry behind the first sighting of the crescent moon. Sanusi explains the concepts of the 'circle of vision' and 'circle of light' and how their coincidence and subsequent separation lead to the appearance of a thin illuminated arc. You will see how the author uses a fixed Earth‑centered model to clarify a phenomenon observed by everyone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on crescent formation
Read the Arabic passage that describes the coincidence of the two circles and the emergence of the crescent. Follow the translation to ensure you grasp the geometric scenario.
The passage assumes the moon is a sphere lit by the sun; the circle of vision is the boundary between what we see and what we don't.
2
Identify the two key circles
Notice the definition of 'دائرة الرؤية' (circle of vision) and 'دائرة الضوء' (circle of light). Understand why they coincide at the beginning of the month.
The circle of light is the terminator; the circle of vision is the limb from Earth's perspective.
3
Visualize the intersection
Imagine the moon moving away from the sun. The circles separate, and a thin arc of the illuminated side becomes visible. This arc is the crescent (الهلال).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثاني : المقصد الأول : القمر واستضاءته من الشمس
وجهه المضيء إليها دوننا فلا نرى له ضوءاً أصلاً ( وكانت) حينئذ ( دائرة الرؤية) وهي الدائرة الفاصلة بين المرئي وغير المرئي منه ( منطبقة على دائرة الضوء وهي ) الدائرة ( الفاصلة بين المضيء والمظلم منه ونفرض دائرة الرؤية ثابتة و ) نقول : ( دائرة الضوء تزول ) لا محالة ( بزواله ) أي تزول بزوال القمر ) عن المسامتة ) أي المقارنة للشمس ) فبعد الانفراج بينهما ) أي بعد زوال الانطباق وحصول الانكشاف والتقاطع بين الدائرتين يقع شيء من الوجه المضيء مستدق بين نصفيهما وحينئذ ( نرى قوسا من الوجه المضيء إلينا فهذا المرئي هو الهلال ) ولا يزال ذلك يكبر بالبعد عن الشمس ويزداد المرئي من الوجه المضيء عظماً ) حتى يصير الوجه المضيء) بتمامه ( إلينا ) وذلك عند المقابلة التي هي غاية البعد بينهما ( و ) حينئذ ( ينطبق الدائرتان مرة أخرى فنراه (بدراً كاملاً كدائرة تامة ( ثم) إن النيرين بعد غاية البعد بينهما ( يتقاربان ) من الجانب الآخر ( فتتقاطع ) حينئذ ( الدائرتان ) مرة أخرى ( وينحرف عنا شيء مستدق من الوجه ( المضيء ) فينتقص كمال البدرية، وهكذا ينحرف المضيء شيئاً فشيئاً (حتى ترى منه شكلاً هلالياً في جانب المشرق ثم ( يخفى بالكلية وهو المحاق وإنما لا نرى) القمر ( يوماً وأكثر بعد المقارنة وقبلها لضعف ضوئه ودقته وقربه من الشمس مع ضوئها ) الغالب الساتر لما يقرب منها (فيمتنع) القمر لهذه الأسباب ) من إبصاره) وأما إذا كان بعيداً عنها في أحد جانبيها بمقدار اثنتي عشرة درجة فإنه يرى عادة مستمرة وربما نرى بأقل منها فإن ذلك مما يختلف بحسب عرض القمر وصفاء الأفق وقوة الباصرة .
كذا في نهاية الإدراك ووجه ضعفه منع الملازمة لجواز أن يكون لكل جزء من أجزاء القمر نسبة إلى كل جزء من الشمس يوجب الانعكاس على جميع أجزائه، ومنع لبطلان التالي بجواز أن يكون بعض أجزائه منيراً ويرى الكل منيراً لبعده .
قوله : ( فنصفه المقابل إلخ) أي نصفه التقريبي لما بين أن الكرة الصغرى إذا قبل النور من كرة كبرى كان المضيء أكثر من النصف . قوله : (منطبقة) أي تقريباً لأن المرئي منه أقل من النصف والمستضيء أكثر منه .
قوله : ( ويزداد المرئي من الوجه المضيء عظماً) فعند تمام التربيع من الشمس كانت الدائرتان متقاطعتان على حادة ومنفرجة إلى أن يتطابقا عند مقارنة أحدهما للآخر أو عند مقابلته له .
First Observatory - Second Section: First Intention: The Moon and Its Illumination from the Sun
Its illuminated face towards it and not towards us, so we do not see any light at all. (And it was) then (the circle of vision) which is the dividing circle between the visible and non-visible part of it (coincident with the circle of light, which is the) circle (dividing between its illuminated and dark part. And we assume the circle of vision is fixed, and) we say: (the circle of light disappears) inevitably (with its removal), i.e., it disappears with the removal of the moon (from the alignment), i.e., the proximity to the sun. (So after the separation between them), i.e., after the removal of coincidence and the occurrence of opening and intersection between the two circles, a portion of the illuminated face falls, thin between their halves, and then (we see an arc of the illuminated face towards us, so this visible thing is the crescent). And it continues to grow with distance from the sun, and the visible part of the illuminated face increases in size (until the entire illuminated face becomes towards us), and that is at opposition which is the utmost distance between them. (And) then (the two circles coincide again, and we see it as a full moon like a complete circle. (Then) after the utmost distance between the two luminaries (they approach) from the other side, (so the two circles intersect) again (and a thin portion of the illuminated face turns away from us, so the perfection of fullness decreases, and thus the illuminated part turns away little by little until you see from it a crescent shape on the eastern side, then it disappears completely, and that is the new moon. And we do not see) the moon (for a day or more after and before the conjunction because of the weakness of its light and its fineness and its proximity to the sun with its light which predominates and covers what is near it. So the moon, for these reasons, is prevented from being seen. But when it is far from it on one of its sides by about twelve degrees, it is seen continuously by custom, and perhaps we see it at less than that, for that varies according to the moon's latitude, clarity of the horizon, and strength of the eyesight.
Thus in Nihayat al-Idrak, and the weakness of its proof is the denial of the necessary connection because it is possible that for each part of the moon there is a proportion to each part of the sun that necessitates reflection on all its parts, and the denial of the falsity of the consequent because it is possible that some of its parts are illuminated and the whole is seen as illuminated due to its distance.
His saying: ((so its opposite half etc.)) i.e., its approximate half, because it was explained that when the smaller sphere receives light from a larger sphere, the illuminated part is more than half. His saying: (coincident) i.e., approximately, because the visible part of it is less than half and the illuminated part is more than it.
His saying: ((and the visible part of the illuminated face increases in size)) for at the completion of quadrature from the sun, the two circles were intersecting at acute and obtuse angles until they coincide at the conjunction of one with the other or at its opposition.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the astronomical basis for the moon's phases, particularly the crescent (hilal) and its visibility. It begins by describing the geometry: the moon's illuminated side faces the sun, so when the moon is near the sun, its illuminated side is away from us, and we see no light. The 'circle of vision' (the boundary between the visible and invisible hemisphere from Earth) coincides with the 'circle of light' (the terminator) at conjunction. As the moon separates from the sun, the circles intersect, revealing a thin crescent. This crescent grows until full moon at opposition, then wanes. The author then addresses why the moon is not seen for a day or more after conjunction: because its light is weak, its disk is thin, and it is too close to the brilliant sun. Visibility depends on distance (about 12 degrees) and atmospheric/optical conditions. A reference to Nihayat al-Idrak notes a possible objection: that each part of the moon might reflect from all parts of the sun, but this is rejected. Finally, the glosses clarify that the visible half is slightly less than the half due to perspective, and that at quadrature the circles intersect at acute/obtuse angles until coincidence at conjunction or opposition.

Key Terms
هلال
دائرة الرؤية
دائرة الضوء
مقارنة
انطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The crescent is caused by Earth's shadow falling on the moon.
Correction: The passage shows the crescent is purely due to the geometry of the moon's illuminated face relative to Earth; Earth's shadow is not involved.

Checkpoint
What are the two circles that coincide at the beginning of the month?
A) Circle of light and circle of darkness
B) Circle of vision and circle of light
C) Circle of crescent and circle of full moon
D) Circle of Earth and circle of moon

Key Takeaway
The crescent moon is the first visible arc of the illuminated part of the moon after it separates from the sun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen countless crescents, but have you ever paused to reflect on the precise divine order that brings it into view at the exact moment it does? This very moon determines when you fast Ramadan and when you celebrate Eid. Will you now look at the crescent with the awe it deserves, and let it remind you that your worship is timed by a cosmos that obeys its Creator?
?
The crescent appears only when the circles of vision and light intersect—a moment of perfect geometry. Your own life is filled with intersections of divine decree and your choices. Are you aware of the precise moments when Allah's light intersects with your vision, guiding you to action? Will you train yourself to see those intersections as clearly as the crescent, and respond before they pass?
?
This lesson has shown you that the moon's phases are not random but follow a fixed law. The start of Ramadan is not guesswork; it is a matter of waiting until the crescent is seen. How seriously do you take the actual sighting of the moon? Do you dismiss it as a formality, or do you recognize it as a moment when the entire Muslim community aligns itself with the precise command of Allah? Will you commit to knowing the moon's phases better so that you can anticipate and prepare for the worship it initiates?

From Full Moon to New Moon
Sanusi describes the full moon and waning phases using the circle model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the progression from full moon to waning crescent and new moon as described by the continuous intersection of the circles.

Core Idea
The moon's phases are a continuous change in the intersection of the two circles.

This lesson follows the moon's journey from opposition (full moon) back to conjunction (new moon). The author explains how the illuminated face becomes fully visible at full moon, then gradually turns away, producing waning phases until a thin crescent reappears on the eastern side before complete disappearance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the lunar cycle
Read the Arabic from the full moon description to the end of the main explanation (before the objection). Notice the symmetry with the waxing phase.
The passage uses the same geometric model in reverse.
2
Identify the full moon condition
At opposition, the two circles coincide again, but this time the entire illuminated face is turned toward Earth, giving a full moon.
Opposition means the moon is opposite the sun relative to Earth.
3
Trace the waning phase
As the moon approaches the sun from the other side, the circles intersect and the illuminated portion recedes, creating a crescent on the eastern side before it disappears.
The crescent appears on the opposite side compared to the waxing crescent.
4
Define the new moon (محاق)
When the moon is again near the sun, the illuminated side is entirely away, and we see nothing. This is the new moon (محاق).
The term 'محاق' literally means 'wearing away'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثاني : المقصد الأول : القمر واستضاءته من الشمس
وجهه المضيء إليها دوننا فلا نرى له ضوءاً أصلاً ( وكانت) حينئذ ( دائرة الرؤية) وهي الدائرة الفاصلة بين المرئي وغير المرئي منه ( منطبقة على دائرة الضوء وهي ) الدائرة ( الفاصلة بين المضيء والمظلم منه ونفرض دائرة الرؤية ثابتة و ) نقول : ( دائرة الضوء تزول ) لا محالة ( بزواله ) أي تزول بزوال القمر ) عن المسامتة ) أي المقارنة للشمس ) فبعد الانفراج بينهما ) أي بعد زوال الانطباق وحصول الانكشاف والتقاطع بين الدائرتين يقع شيء من الوجه المضيء مستدق بين نصفيهما وحينئذ ( نرى قوسا من الوجه المضيء إلينا فهذا المرئي هو الهلال ) ولا يزال ذلك يكبر بالبعد عن الشمس ويزداد المرئي من الوجه المضيء عظماً ) حتى يصير الوجه المضيء) بتمامه ( إلينا ) وذلك عند المقابلة التي هي غاية البعد بينهما ( و ) حينئذ ( ينطبق الدائرتان مرة أخرى فنراه (بدراً كاملاً كدائرة تامة ( ثم) إن النيرين بعد غاية البعد بينهما ( يتقاربان ) من الجانب الآخر ( فتتقاطع ) حينئذ ( الدائرتان ) مرة أخرى ( وينحرف عنا شيء مستدق من الوجه ( المضيء ) فينتقص كمال البدرية، وهكذا ينحرف المضيء شيئاً فشيئاً (حتى ترى منه شكلاً هلالياً في جانب المشرق ثم ( يخفى بالكلية وهو المحاق وإنما لا نرى) القمر ( يوماً وأكثر بعد المقارنة وقبلها لضعف ضوئه ودقته وقربه من الشمس مع ضوئها ) الغالب الساتر لما يقرب منها (فيمتنع) القمر لهذه الأسباب ) من إبصاره) وأما إذا كان بعيداً عنها في أحد جانبيها بمقدار اثنتي عشرة درجة فإنه يرى عادة مستمرة وربما نرى بأقل منها فإن ذلك مما يختلف بحسب عرض القمر وصفاء الأفق وقوة الباصرة .
كذا في نهاية الإدراك ووجه ضعفه منع الملازمة لجواز أن يكون لكل جزء من أجزاء القمر نسبة إلى كل جزء من الشمس يوجب الانعكاس على جميع أجزائه، ومنع لبطلان التالي بجواز أن يكون بعض أجزائه منيراً ويرى الكل منيراً لبعده .
قوله : ( فنصفه المقابل إلخ) أي نصفه التقريبي لما بين أن الكرة الصغرى إذا قبل النور من كرة كبرى كان المضيء أكثر من النصف . قوله : (منطبقة) أي تقريباً لأن المرئي منه أقل من النصف والمستضيء أكثر منه .
قوله : ( ويزداد المرئي من الوجه المضيء عظماً) فعند تمام التربيع من الشمس كانت الدائرتان متقاطعتان على حادة ومنفرجة إلى أن يتطابقا عند مقارنة أحدهما للآخر أو عند مقابلته له .
First Observatory - Second Section: First Intention: The Moon and Its Illumination from the Sun
Its illuminated face towards it and not towards us, so we do not see any light at all. (And it was) then (the circle of vision) which is the dividing circle between the visible and non-visible part of it (coincident with the circle of light, which is the) circle (dividing between its illuminated and dark part. And we assume the circle of vision is fixed, and) we say: (the circle of light disappears) inevitably (with its removal), i.e., it disappears with the removal of the moon (from the alignment), i.e., the proximity to the sun. (So after the separation between them), i.e., after the removal of coincidence and the occurrence of opening and intersection between the two circles, a portion of the illuminated face falls, thin between their halves, and then (we see an arc of the illuminated face towards us, so this visible thing is the crescent). And it continues to grow with distance from the sun, and the visible part of the illuminated face increases in size (until the entire illuminated face becomes towards us), and that is at opposition which is the utmost distance between them. (And) then (the two circles coincide again, and we see it as a full moon like a complete circle. (Then) after the utmost distance between the two luminaries (they approach) from the other side, (so the two circles intersect) again (and a thin portion of the illuminated face turns away from us, so the perfection of fullness decreases, and thus the illuminated part turns away little by little until you see from it a crescent shape on the eastern side, then it disappears completely, and that is the new moon. And we do not see) the moon (for a day or more after and before the conjunction because of the weakness of its light and its fineness and its proximity to the sun with its light which predominates and covers what is near it. So the moon, for these reasons, is prevented from being seen. But when it is far from it on one of its sides by about twelve degrees, it is seen continuously by custom, and perhaps we see it at less than that, for that varies according to the moon's latitude, clarity of the horizon, and strength of the eyesight.
Thus in Nihayat al-Idrak, and the weakness of its proof is the denial of the necessary connection because it is possible that for each part of the moon there is a proportion to each part of the sun that necessitates reflection on all its parts, and the denial of the falsity of the consequent because it is possible that some of its parts are illuminated and the whole is seen as illuminated due to its distance.
His saying: ((so its opposite half etc.)) i.e., its approximate half, because it was explained that when the smaller sphere receives light from a larger sphere, the illuminated part is more than half. His saying: (coincident) i.e., approximately, because the visible part of it is less than half and the illuminated part is more than it.
His saying: ((and the visible part of the illuminated face increases in size)) for at the completion of quadrature from the sun, the two circles were intersecting at acute and obtuse angles until they coincide at the conjunction of one with the other or at its opposition.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the astronomical basis for the moon's phases, particularly the crescent (hilal) and its visibility. It begins by describing the geometry: the moon's illuminated side faces the sun, so when the moon is near the sun, its illuminated side is away from us, and we see no light. The 'circle of vision' (the boundary between the visible and invisible hemisphere from Earth) coincides with the 'circle of light' (the terminator) at conjunction. As the moon separates from the sun, the circles intersect, revealing a thin crescent. This crescent grows until full moon at opposition, then wanes. The author then addresses why the moon is not seen for a day or more after conjunction: because its light is weak, its disk is thin, and it is too close to the brilliant sun. Visibility depends on distance (about 12 degrees) and atmospheric/optical conditions. A reference to Nihayat al-Idrak notes a possible objection: that each part of the moon might reflect from all parts of the sun, but this is rejected. Finally, the glosses clarify that the visible half is slightly less than the half due to perspective, and that at quadrature the circles intersect at acute/obtuse angles until coincidence at conjunction or opposition.

بدر
محاق
مقابلة
تربيع
هلال

Checkpoint
When do we see a full moon according to the passage?
A) When the moon is closest to the sun
B) When the moon is at opposition (farthest from the sun)
C) When the circles first intersect
D) When the moon is at quadrature

Key Takeaway
The moon's phases from full to new are a symmetric reversal of the waxing process, ending in complete invisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The moon’s fullness does not last; it wanes until nothing remains. In what area of your life are you still in the 'full moon' phase of pride or strength, refusing to see the waning that has already begun? What will you do today to prepare for that inevitable decrease?
?
You read that the illuminated face turns away little by little until only a thin crescent remains. What in your own soul is still turned away from Allah, hidden even from yourself? How will you turn it back before you reach your own 'new moon'?
?
The full moon happens when the circles coincide perfectly—complete alignment. Is there any part of your life where you are perfectly aligned with Allah’s will right now? If not, which one misalignment causes the most loss of light, and what is your first step to correct it today?

Visibility Conditions and Objection
Sanusi explains moon visibility conditions and rebuts an alternative reflection theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the moon is not seen for a day or more after conjunction and how an alternative theory is refuted.

Core Idea
The moon is invisible near the sun due to weak light and overwhelming sunlight, not because it lacks illumination.

This lesson addresses a practical question: why don't we see the moon for a day or so after new moon? Sanusi gives three reasons: weak light, thin crescent, and proximity to the bright sun. He then engages with an objection from Nihayat al-Idrak that suggests an alternative explanation based on reflection, and clarifies why the moon's visible portion is slightly less than half.

Guided Path
1
Read the visibility explanation
Read the Arabic passage that lists the reasons for the moon's invisibility after conjunction. Understand each factor.
The reasons are: ضعف ضوئه, دقته, قربه من الشمس with its غالب ساتر.
2
Note the 12‑degree rule
The author mentions that when the moon is about 12 degrees away from the sun, it is usually visible. This is a practical astronomical rule.
Visibility also depends on lunar latitude, atmospheric clarity, and eyesight strength.
3
Examine the objection from Nihayat al-Idrak
The objection claims that each part of the moon reflects from all parts of the sun, so the whole moon should appear illuminated. Sanusi rejects this by denying the necessary connection.
The objection is based on a different theory of reflection; Sanusi's rebuttal relies on the geometry of the circles.
4
Understand the gloss about 'approximate half'
The gloss clarifies that the visible half is less than half and the illuminated part more than half, due to the sun being a larger sphere.
This explains why at quadrature the circles intersect at acute/obtuse angles.

?

Today, when you see the crescent moon, will you pause to reflect on the divine wisdom in its gradual reappearance? Resolve to recite the dua of seeing the moon and reflect on its lesson of patience and clarity.
